viii



INTRODUCTION
The study of myths invites several alternative methods of approach. Perhaps the easiest is to take every story as falling into the category of children's tales or of incredible religious origins, without any endeavour to relate it to the general scheme of events. Another is to consider all myth as symbolical, in which case the identification of the symbols will vary from solar deities to aspects of good and evil according to the fashion of the period. The method adopted in this work has been to endeavour to relate these tales to the shadowy beginnings of history, in the hope that it will eventually be possible to fit them into the threedimensional jig-saw puzzle formed by our cultural, historical, and religious background. It must frankly be admitted that progress is slow and that the desired aim will not be attained for several generations, but it is felt that the establishing of the principle and the small beginnings here shown are essential as a foundation. Myth may best be described as the gossamer cloak of folk memory overlaying the bare bones of pre-history. In attempting to understand it, consideration should be given to the possibility that primitive man in telling these stories was not guilty of deliberate misrepresentation but rather that owing to his inability to perceive the relationship between cause and effect, or to distinguish between fact and prejudice, we are presented in them with a series of glimpses of history as seen through the eyes of children. While the dating of myths is still extremely difficult, the following tentative sequence of mythical patterns may form a useful standard of reference. The oldest stories which have come to us appear to be the creation myths. This is not because primitive man could be expected to remember the actual creation, but because the majority of these stories refer to tire emergence of humanity into a new' life after some great catastrophe, an occurrence of such magnitude to the narrators that the mere fact of survival seemed like a rebirth. Their genuineness is shown by the fact that they date back to periods when philosophic or scientific doctrines as to the beginning of things had not yet been evolved. Those which are of later date frequently bear the imprint of the abstract religious philosophies of the Hellenistic schools of the Middle East and should be considered as theological conceptions rather than as true myths.
IX



INTRODUCTION
Manv creation inytlis date back to tbe period when men lived
in caves, although with few exceptions the stories referring to cave
life come from the Americas. There is a distinct possibility that
these caverns were places of refuge rather than favoured dwelling
places, but until further work has been done on the interpretation of the petroglyphs and cave paintings, in their relationship to
myth and legend, this question must remain open.
The hundreds of deluge and fire myths all belong to this period, and can usually be fitted into the following pattern:
1. The Tribal Deity is displeased with humanity and threatens to punish them by driving them from the Elysian Fields or earthly
paradise. 2. There are signs and portents in the heavens, such as the changing of the course of one or more of the heavenly bodies. 3. The Ancestor (man or woman) is warned of approaching disaster, and advised to flee to some high mountain, to a place of refuge, or to build a ship. 4. He does so and is mocked by the wicked and impious. 5. The time of trial begins. There are cyclones and hurricanes, quantities of rocks and stones falling from above, volcanic eruptions, followed by vast fires, which are only extinguished by the heavy rains and the uprising of the waters of the great deep, which cause a flood on a vast scale. 6. The waters eventually drain away and the ancestor sets about the founding of a new race. In order to do this he is forced to resort to incest or other desperate expedients. Many
legends tend to begin at this point—presumably because there were
only a few survivors but no leader—with the Deity surveying the
watery waste and considering ways and means of beginning again. In the Americas there are several stories of survivors digging themselves out of caves which have fallen in. 7. Some myths give details of the re-establishment of life and even go so far as to provide genealogical trees Unking historical rulers with the great ancestors.
Following after the creation myths come the stories of the mother goddesses, dating back to the matriarchal system of Ufe,
when the wise woman of the clan or tribe was just beginning that evolution which reached its zenith when she was the titular representative of the Great Mother, and with her college of priestesses attended the annual or half-yearly fertility festivals, at which her last consort was slain and eucharistically devoured
or her new consort was chosen and together with his cohort
ritually fathered the next generation of royal children. The
stories of virgin birth originate in this period and seem merely to
x



INTRODUCTION
refer to the results of ritual cohabitation when the father remained unknown and the ranlc and inheritance came through the mother.
The first, mythological trinity may have consisted of mother, daughter, and grand-daughter, the change over to mother, father, and son coming at a later period. The nadir of the mother
goddess was reached when her college of attendant priestesses had
become the covens of popular witchcraft. An interesting corollary
of this process was the change in sex of the early trinity of Moon Father, Sun Mother, and Venus Son to the present Sun Father, Moon Mother, and Venus Daughter, a process which had not been
completed in the Hadramaut at the end of the First World War (in Germanic languages the moon is still of masculine gender). It does not fall within the province of this work to follow up the supersession of the early Trinity myths by the religions of to-day, but nevertheless this should not be overlooked : there is scarcely a religious ritual of any of the present-day churches which has not its roots in the far distant past. The fact that many goddesses, particularly in Egypt, assumed the character of the animal totem of their clan, which was in some cases also a sign of the zodiac, would place the switch over from the mother goddesses to the father gods in the Nile Valley at about the time of the invention of the zodiac, which must have been some time before the Sothic Cycle which began in 4326 B.c. That this change coincided with that from nomadic pastoral life to the more settled agricultural existence is reasonably certain, but it does not seem clear why certain major gods changed their sex and others transferred their functions. The conception of non-material deities is of recent date, all the early gods and goddesses having been regarded as endowed with human qualities to a superhuman degree. These early divinities were lusty beings, the dynamic progenitors of races, the leaders of armies, and the sources of wisdom and knowledge, and their vitality was in marked contrast to the pallidity of their successors. In considering the reasons for the change from maternal to paternal gods, it must be recollected that the Semitic tribes in and around the Fertile Crescent have always shown the greatest antipathy to granting women any semblance of equality whether on earth or in heaven, an attitude typified by Saint Paul and later by
Mohammed. The continued debased position of women in the
Arab world to-day shows that this point of view still holds good, although it is still not clear whether the reason is racial, climatic,
or merely pathological.
M
i tli the eclipse of the matriarchy the scene was set for the appearance of the culture hero, and for his rise from the status of a
mere tribal chieftain to being the personal representative of the xi



INTRODUCTION
high gods and, later, to a place amongst them. The elucidation
of the myths of this period is much hampered by our lack of know
ledge whether the names which have come down to us are heredi
tarv titles, military or other ranks, or even nicknames, and also
whether thcv applied to an individual, a family, a tribe, or even a
dynasty. There is also some difficulty in ascertaining which
divine titles and heroic deeds were taken over en bloc from the
tribes whom they defeated and which were at the same time relegated to the powers of evil. It would seem that the theory- of dualism, whereby every- well
disposed divinity is balanced by an evilly intentioned one, may liave had its origin in the need to nullify the powers of the gods or
heroes of a conquered race, or alternatively it may be linked to the taboo against twin children. The idea of the supreme god sitting at the head of a conference table has persisted until the present day, the writer having in his possession a recent Egyptian colour print showing a supreme being surrounded by alternate good and bad minor divinities. The transfer of functions of the gods of the vanquished has had some strange results. For example, Shaitan, the culture hero of some aboriginal tribe living in the Arabian Desert, has in the course of ages become first the archetype of the third species of jinn, and later the evil one of the Christian Church. The Devas, the gods of the pre-Yedic tribes of India, became the powers of evil of the Zoroastrians while remaining the powers of good of the Vedas. In the same manner Set, the original sun god of Upper Egypt, after the unification of Upper and Lower Egypt, became the chief of the powers of evil, as opposed to Osiris, the leader of the forces of good. Throughout all this period mythology and comparative theology are intermixed, probably because the priest, the king, and the judge were usually the same person, so no conflict of interests was likely to occur. Nowadays all is changed and the most sacred rituals of the past have become children’s games, mimes, or folk dances. It was a writer of the last century who pointed out that the traditional harlequinade, with which the Christmas pantomime used always to finish, was no more than a degenerate representation of the great ones of the past. Mercury, Pan, Diana, and Zeus
being metamorphosed into Harlequin, Pantaloon, Columbine, and
Clown.
European myth has suffered greatly from the impact of Christianity . It is axiomatic that it is only in countries where the myths
vere actually put into writing before the arrival of missionaries
that they retained any real value for the student. For example,
the Edda of the Scandinavians was put into writing while the old
xii



INTRODUCTION
religion still existed, and is therefore a most valuable source. The Arthurian Legends, on tire other hand, were put into writing
after the conversion of the Celts, and have so much extraneous matter introduced that it is difficult to detect underneath it all the stories of Gwydion and other early Celtic heroes, and to realize that the King Arthur who fought the Saxons and the heroes whose deeds make up the cycle of the Round Table probably never heard of each other. The same process is to be noted in the Teutonic versions of the Eddie poems, and particularly, in the Slavonic tales, where the art of writing was brought in by the missionaries and the original background of myth is almost lost. It is a tragic reflection that the zeal of the great ones of the Church has only had the effect of destroying the records of cultures that had existed for much longer before them than the present culture is likely to exist after them. In the Americas several of the pre-Columbian races had evolved a form of writing and had produced whole libraries of picture codices telling of the history of the various tribes. Unfortunately the Spaniards on their arrival, possibly owing to their fear of cultures which were as steeped in blood as their own, destroyed every document which they could find, with the result that nobody has yet succeeded in reading the glyphs of the Mayas, with the exception of dates, and it is improbable that any one will manage to do so. One of the results of this situation is that it is not always possible to match up the tales of the heroes which have come down to us by word of mouth with either the stone carvings which are so prolifically dotted all over the American continent, or even those depicted in the scant dozen codices which escaped the fury of the priests. It has been possible to go fairly deeply into Egyptian myth, thanks to the fact that the ancient Egyptians left behind not only many statues and mural drawings, but also the famous Pyramid Texts and later vast quantities of papyri, vttli the aid of which it has been possible to identify the great majority of their gods and heroes. That the influence of the ancient faith is not yet dead is shown by Egyptian women in the Delta who are in labour
calling on Amoon (Amon) for help, a practice which occurred as late as 1945, giving this religion an effective life of well over six thousand years. Had it not been for its tragic disintegration between 600 b.c. and 450 B.c. under the Greek influence, we should doubtless have had access to many more records of Egypt’s past.
The myths of Mesopotamia are nearly as old as those of Egypt.
The number that have come down to us is, however, far less owing
to the fact that the mud brick of which everything in the Tigris
xiii



INTRODUCTION
Euphrates valleys was built returns to dust after having been
baked in the sun for thousands of years, while the stone blocks of
Egypt arc still much the same as when they were placed in
position. The Tablets of the Creation from the library of King Ashurbanipal, now in the British Ivluscum, are, nevertheless, fuller
than anything which has come down to us from Egypt. These
stories were incredibly old when they were copied in ooo "B.c. and
appear to have been drawn from the same source as that used by
the Hebrews and Phoenicians, while they may also have been
known in pre-dynastic Egypt.
The Renaissance brought to the knowledge of the western
world the treasuries of Greek and Roman myth, to become the inspiration for poetry and art and the backbone of classical education. It is a matter for regret that no such impetus was available for the myths of India, and that Indian studies have produced no Pauly Wissowa to assist in disentangling the original stories from the two hundred and twenty thousand lines of the Maliabharata, the ninety-six thousand lines of the Ramayana, and other large-scale epics. If the tendencies towards multilimbed gods and goddesses, vast numbers of years outrunning the longest time scales for the existence of the earth, and the accentuation of the procreative motif are any indication, the Hindu religion is now in the full flood of decay and in a few short generations there will no longer be any fresh material on the glorious past of the Vcdic races available for the student. The myths of Africa and Australasia have suffered greatly from the well-meant intentions of missionaries and educationists and in the last hundred years most of them have already been forgotten, a process assisted by tbe inability of the average tribesman to commit his stories to writing. Doubtless the social psychologist and the anthropologist may manage to extract a few more details, but in general the future will lie mainly in the intensive study of material already to hand. While the work of the mythographer is not always as spectacular as that of the archaeologist, it must be remembered that had it not been for the mythical tales of Troy, Schliemann would never have dug there and another chapter of history would have remained closed. The study of myths establishes a link between prehistory and history', emphasizes that all gods were once men, and
shows that the marvellous element in these stories is mainly due to misunderstanding, although it is just possible that an occasional
forerunner df modem inventions may have made a spasmodic and
short-lived appearance. Ihe kinship of myths originating from adjacent and even
distant countries shows that the diffusion of cultures was a marked xiv



INTRODUCTION TO THE THIRD EDITION
factor in the cultural development of mankind in the prehistoric period. This possibility is confirmed by the apparent lack of originality in religions, each of which seems to stem from its predecessor with grafts from contemporary opposing groups. One day perhaps it will be possible to investigate religious systems from a mythical basis; the results of such a study should be of
great interest. Readers will note that what is perhaps the greatest untapped store of myth now existing, the One Thousand and One Nights, being the stories of the merchants and captains who roamed the area between the Mediterranean and the Pacific, long before Islam had been brought into being or Haroun al-Rashid born, has not been broken down for inclusion in the present work. This is regretted, but it would have doubled the size of the present volume, in addition to causing its publication to be deferred for several years.
INTRODUCTION TO THE THIRD EDITION
The gulf which has separated myth, legend, and folklore, on the one hand, and historical, archaeological, and anthropological research on the other, may be said to have narrowed to a considerable extent during the ten years which have elapsed since this dictionary was first written. It is as if students of the past and its mysteries had suddenly discovered that they had had a first-class crib to all the subjects being studied, which had lain neglected at their feet for years, while all the time it contained the answers to practically all the questions they were asking. Naturally it was galling to some to have to seek for knowledge from stories which had, in many cases, arrived at a stage of being only found on the nursery bookshelf, but this should not have been so as the true scientist takes his knowledge where he can find it and is not snobbish about its origins.
We have now reached a stage in the world’s history where the untapped sources of historical raw material are restricted to three or four groups. These are the Orthodox churches of Russia, many of which have libraries which are only now coming to light; Mus
lim and Christian foundations in the Near East; Buddhist libraries in Tibet and China ; and finall}' the large stores of books available
xv



INTRODUCTION TO THE THIRD EDITION
in India which nobody has got down to even reading, much less
translating. Here will doubtless be found the travel diaries of early sea
captains together with their maps, the poems composed in honour of victor}' or defeat, the stories of gods and heroes as told for popular consumption, and other bits of valuable information which have somehow managed to escape the blue pencil of the
religious censor.
Recent examples of this have been the Piri Reis map of the Atlantic Ocean drawn up years before Columbus; the Dead Sea Scrolls, which have caused a great shifting of emphasis for the period 200 b . c . to a . d . 200; the recent Soviet investigations on the return route of the Argonauts; the strong relationship in early dynastic times between Egyptian and Slavonic legends; the fascinating linkage between hill figures in Cambridgeshire, the Lady of Coventry who rode on a white horse as also the one who rode with bells on her fingers and bells on her toes, Gog and Magog, and a matriarchal goddess known from Palestine to Ireland. No reference has been made to the Americas as, although the working out of the relationship between such written documents as we still possess, such as the Popul Vuh and the Maya and Aztec scripts, is still forming the subject of careful study, progress has indeed been much slower than was hopefully anticipated a couple of years ago. This state of affairs is partially due to the fact that whilst in Europe and the Near East every scrap of information, ranging from passages of Halley’s Comet to solar and lunar eclipses, is being carefully studied as a means of checking the dates of historical events, this matter has not by any means received adequate attention in the Americas, with the result that any correlation between the calendar of the Mayas and our own is haphazard in the extreme. Another branch that has not received adequate attention is that of travellers' tales. Whether it was Herodotus or Hanno, Sir John
Mandeville or Marco Polo, Adam of Bremen or Benjamin of Tudela, there has always been a tendency to laugh off the stories of the marvels and miracles encountered by them as being figments of the imagination, instead of accepting them as inadequate descriptions of tilings which were outside the range of compre
hension of those who witnessed them.
An example of this occurs in two of the early Irish voyages,
when it was reported that an island had been seen supported by a
large pillar on which it rested. This has been assumed by most
people to be on the level of Baron Munchliausen. But to anybody
"ho has ever seen the mushroom effect of a volcano in eruption or
of an atomic bomb being exploded, the effect is precisely that of xvi



INTRODUCTION TO THE THIRD EDITION
an island supported on a huge pillar. The fact that the truth as seen by these early mariners was not immediately comprehensible does not imply that they were not telling the truth but merely that thenreaders had not taken the trouble to use logical analysis. That several ethnic groups, including that of the Aryan language
peoples, share a common tradition of myth and legend is well
known to many people, but there is uncertainty as to why and when they were forced apart by circumstances.
A possible explanation lies in the fact that during the period of
the last ice age, when much of Europe and Asia was covered by sheets of ice up to a mile in thickness, the nations sharing this common culture lived sufficiently far from the lower edges to enjoy reasonably good climatic conditions. However, when the ice age came to an end, the sudden melting of vast quantities of ice produced flood waters which turned a large portion of Asia into a series of inland seas, stretching from the Gobi to the Black Sea. While it is not possible to date this with certainty it may be observed that the last great climatic change took place about ioooo B.c., and that by a coincidence the common starting-off points of four of the main calendrical systems of the world, those of Chaldea, Egypt, the Mayas, Vedic India, can be set between 11542 b.c. for the first two and 11653 B.c. f°r the second pair. In addition the date on which the star Anna had been in the 'sanctuary' on 2nd February, the date on which Our Lord was presented in the Temple, has been calculated by Schiaparelli as being in 1x230 b.c. In the year of Our Lord the date was 25th July. It is precisely the differences between these dates which give them the possibility of reality, as it may be taken that after emigrating to a new land the race would take the date of their arrival there as a suitable one for a new calendar. This does not imply that the Mayas, the Egyptians, and the Israelis need have come from the same area but merely that they were equally affected by the worldwide climatic change. With this in the background it is easier to appreciate why most of the cultures with which historians have come into contact show a far greater degree of similarity in their backgrounds than might otherwise have been expected. The tendency on the part of the great research workers of the last century to assume that each race had its separate and peculiar cultural origin has been shown to be based on a fallacy. Modern research shows the reverse to have been the case, that no race can be shown to have experienced any series of cosmic or world catastrophes, or of religious or cultural backgrounds, which is markedly different from that of any other one.



INTRODUCTION TO THE THIRD EDITION
From the date of 11500 b.c. given above it may be said that our
history actuallv begins. For as long as the waters continued to
pour across Asia communication by ship was possible from one end of Asia to the other, and it is in the closing stages of this period that we have such stories of piratical expeditions, such as that of the Argonauts, seeking to loot the wealth of a culture infinitely older than their own. It is possible also that the tales of Sindbad, or of Yamilka, or of the Shipwrecked Sailor, belong to this period.
With the drying up of the waters communication by water ceased, and the land trade routes for amber, furs, silk, spices began to be developed, thus aiding in the prosperity of the petty sovereigns whose kingdoms were enabled to extort a levy on all goods passing through. Gradually, with the building up of minor empires, trade was brought to a standstill, and the manufacture of silk and of swords was brought from the East to Damascus, while amber, being a natural product, became scarce, and Irish blue beads ceased to be exported to such places as Egypt and the Near East. But in spite of all this the memories of the past still remained alive. Haroun al-Raschid, around whom the group of tales known as the Arabian Nights was grouped, was as separated from the period as was the historical King Arthur from the Arthurian Legends. The advent of Christianity resulted in the Pagan gods and heroes being turned into saints, and their deeds revised in accordance with local religious tradition. Nowadays, however, the chromium plate of sanctity is wearing tliin, and underlying it all it is possible to observe the outlines of the original stories. But it must be admitted that we are only at the beginning of the recasting of history in the light of myth and legend, there are generations of work to be done on the subject before we shall have an accurate and valid picture of the past. The process involved in dealing with myth and legend is the simple one of assuming that the narrator told the truth as he saw it. It then becomes a question of a patient search to find a set of facts which give a reasonable correspondence to the story. In doing this it is essential to bear in mind that most early travellers
had been subjected to a Pavlovian preconditioning before their departure, in the anticipation of finding the terrestrial paradise, the fountain of youth, etc., and were, in consequence, likely to trim their accounts to fit in with this. However, truth is like some of the modem plastics, no matter
how much it is deformed by pressure, it always tends to return to its original shape. For example, several of the early Atlantic
travellers told of an island from whence swarms of enormous ants
rushed into the water intent on seizing the ship and its crew. xviii



INTRODUCTION TO THE THIRD EDITION
Many years later the late Professor Rendel Harris pointed out that on approaching the Azores from the East one ran into a series of
dangerous reefs known as the Formigas, or Ant Rocks, a name by which they were known long before the Portuguese officially discovered the islands. And, what is more, Rendel Harris also observed that to an Anglo-Saxon mariner the rendering of Formigas would be the Antilla or Ant Island. This logical reasoning at one stroke cleared up the identity of a mysterious island which had figured on charts of the Atlantic for centuries. It is an unfortunate fact that the relationship between master mariners and early writers was practically nonexistent. As a result there were two parallel streams of information about foreign lands : the stories told by the men who had actually been there for trade or piracy, and the stories of those who had never been there but who knew how to write. The combination between the two produced results which were frequently ludicrous. One remembers being asked by a Persian in Teheran if the Queen Elizabeth was as big as a motor-bus, and by an Egyptian why snow was white and whether one could not keep it. In both cases the difficulty was simply that the Persian had never seen the sea, much less a ship, whilst the Egyptian had never been in a cold region where snow actually fell. The tendency to fasten on to the careers of famous sailors or soldiers a whole series of events, many of which happened years before they were born or after they died, is not confined to myth and legend. Both Drake and Hawkins would have had to live for centuries to do all that has been attributed to them, while the Mayflower must have been larger than several Atlantic liners to carry all the people who were supposed to have been on board. And here we are dealing with recent historical events where verification should be a simple matter. However, we may adopt the following as a rough basis for future research. Every national epic is designed to elevate the warriors, culture, and religion of the race concerned above all others. Defeats are minimized or overlooked, alien religions are vilified and traduced, successful generals have all the victories in a series of campaigns attributed to them, the virtues of rulers are magnified and their vices forgotten. In short they are propaganda designed to restore flagging morale and may be said to have achieved that aim in nearly every case. In reading the Ramayana with the story of Rustum, or the Eddas together with the Kalevala, one is in the same situation as somebody reading a history of the Napoleonic Wars written by a Frenchman, as against the Journal of Wellington's Campaigns, and a Russian account of the attack on Moscow. With a certain



INTRODUCTION TO THE THIRD EDITION
amount of imagination it is just possible to recognize that they are all three accounts of the same series of events but seen from differing viewpoints. But, nevertheless, to the narrators they all constituted a truthful account of the facts. If we apply the same standard of judgment to all the tales which have come to us from the past the employment of myth and legend to reinforce the shadowy background of history will greatly ease the task of the research worker. E. S. 1961.
Advantage has been taken of the demand for a third edition to enlarge the book by the inclusion of an appendix (pp. 247-63) and two additional illustrations.
xx



BIBLIOGRAPHY
One of the difficulties encountered in delving into non-classical myth and legend is that, apart from the popular collections of tales, the literature on the subject is limited, the great majority of data having to be obtained from the works of specialists in other fields, to whom myth and legend are in the nature of casual interests rather than matter for serious study. The books enumerated in the following bibliography are only pointers to further study and are not to be considered as forming a complete bibliography, which would run into many hundreds of volumes. Many of them are available in cheap editions, the others may be consulted at any good library. In them will be found bibliographies which will assist in the choice of further reading. The wide diversity of the views expressed by the authors should not hinder the student in arriving at a reasonable assessment of the material which is of use to him.

Testament. Various editions; R. Graves: The White Goddess. London, 1948; Rendel Harris: Woodbrooke, Caravan, Sunset, Evergreen, and

of Religion and Ethics. Various editions; A. Hocart: Kings and Councillors. Cairo, 1936; Kingship. London, 1941; S. Reinach: Orpheus. Various editions; L. Spence: Introduction to Mythology. London, 1931;

Note. There are two excellent collections of myths and legends told
for popular reading published by the Gresham Publishing Company of
London, and Messrs. Harrap of London, respectively.
f Dagger Signs. These are used to indicate that there is a separate
entry in the Dictionary for the work marked. In this manner, pro
vided that the reader has even one name in the particular field of mythology which he desires to study, it should be possible to trace,
without undue difficulty, all the other entries in that section.



A
God ‘A.’ The death god of the Mayas.t In the codices he is represented as a being with exposed vertebrae and a skull-like countenance. He may be taken to be the same as Mictlantecuhtli,t the Aztec god of the dead, with the difference that while the Aztec deity presided over the north or south his Mayan counterpart presided over the west. His symbol is that for the day Cimi, ‘death,’ his hieroglyph is a corpse’s head and a skull together with a flint sacrificial knife.
Aah-te-Huti. Egyptian moon god, symbolically represented by an Ibis head surmounted by a crescent and a disk. He was a manifestation of Thoth.t
Ab. In Egyptian religiont the will, the emotion and passion of the Egyptian, symbolized by the heart, which was brought up for judgment, in the Book of the Dead.t
Abac. Irish spelling of Addanc.t
Abtu. In Egyptian myth one of a pair of sacred fish which swam before the boat of Rat to warn him of danger. It also announced the rise of the Nile. The other was Anet.t
Achomawi Indian Creation Legend. This tribe of Californian Indians tell that the Creator originally emerged from a small cloud and that Coyote,t who assisted him, came from a land mist.
Adad or Addu. i. Babylonian god of storm and thunder, forming, with Sint and Shamash,t the second Triad of Gods. Was introduced by the Amonites and later became equated with Rammant or Rimmon, his weapons being flood, famine, and lightning. 2. Canaanite god, also known as Martuyt the Amorite, and Kurgal/f the Great Mountain. Probably the Baalt of Mount Lebanon. The cypress was dedicated to him. 3. Syrian god of virility, known as Hadad.t 4. He may be equated to Buriasht and Teshub.t 5. The Kassite and Hittite gods of storm.
Adapa. A sage of Eridu, second king of Babylonia after the Deluge, and who Berosus says reigned 10,800 years ago. Was initiated into wisdom by Eat although eternal life was withheld frorn him. Once, while fishing, the south wind capsized his boat, and in his fury he broke the wings of the wind, which ceased to blow. Anut summoned him to appear for punishment, and Ea, out of jealousy, warned him not to accept anything to eat or drink. However, both Tammuzt and Gishzidat intervened on his behalf, explaining that Ea had revealed all wisdom to him and that if he but I



Adar Aegir
had eternal life he would be a god. Anu then offered him the bread and water of life but he refused, thus losing for ever immortality for
men. Adapa was the mortal with pure hands who helped the bakers of Eridu to make bread and cleared the holy table of the temple there. Some later versions make Adapa the son of Ea.
Adar. In Babylonian myth an alternative name for Ninib,t the god of the summer sun. Addanc. In Celtic myth a dwarft or marine monster who dwelt
by Lake Llyon Lliont and who caused a deluge. He was eventually
disposed of by being hauled from his lair by the oxen of Hu Gadam,t or alternatively, he was killed by Peredur.t His relationship to this
deluge myth is similar to that of Hayagrivat in Hindu legend. An
alternative spelling is Avane,t whilst in Ireland the word was Abac.t Some further details are given in Celtic Creation Legend.!
Aditya. In Hindu myth the mother of the gods from whom
all things sprang. A personification of the generative powers of nature. Daksha,t her son, was later considered as her father, while her husband Vishnut also appeared as her son when he was incarnated as a dwarf. She was the mother of the Adityas, the gods of the months of the year, and in this connection appears to have taken on the stature of a sun goddess. The twelve Adityas were: Ansa,t Aryman,t Bhaga,t Daksha,t Dhatri,t Indra,t Mitra,t Ravi,t Savitriyf Surya.t Varuna,t and Yama.t Aditya was one of the eight Vasus,t the divine attendants of Indra.t
Adonis. In Babylonian myth a title applied to Tammuz,t derived from Adon meaning lord. The classical use of this name probably arose from a misunderstanding of its meaning.
Adsullata. A river goddess of the continental Celts. The resemblance of the name to Suilat would indicate that she may have been a priestess of hot springs.
Aegir. The ocean god of the Norsemen; although accepted as an equal, he was not one of the Aesir.t He is encountered in three of the Eddas; in the Hymiskvida or Lay of Hymir,t he entertained the Aesir at the feast of the autumnal equinox. His supply of drink being too little for Thor,t the latter sets out with Tyrt to capture Odherir,! the magic cauldron of the giants.! Later in the Aegisdrekka, or Carousal of Aegir, the Aesir drink from the cauldron and are grossly' insulted by Lokrt who slays a servant and flees. 1 his Eddie story is also known as the I.okasenna.t It is to Aegir that Bragif tells the stories enumerated in the Bragi-raedur. Aegir seems to be a pre-Nordic culture hero who was too firmly established to be absorbed or displaced by the Aesir. His wife was named
Rant and they' lived with their nine daughters on the Danish island of Hlesey.
2



Aesir Agassou
Aesir. In Nordic myth the group of leaders forming the entourage of Odin.t Originally there seems to have been a coven of a chief
with a retinue of twelve and it may be assumed that of the seventeen names listed below, never more than a dozen were included at any one time. Their feast day was the Yulet festival of 14th January. The Aesir seem to have arrived in Scandinavia shortly after the
Vanir,! with whom they at first fought and subsequently allied themselves. The stories of their wars against the giants may have related to their struggles with the Finna. The Eddasf tell one side of the story, the Kalevalat and the Hero of Estonia! portions of the
other. Asgard,t the fabled city of the Aesir, with its palaces and assembly halls, may be a memory of their actual capital city before they migrated to north-west Europe. It is possible that some of the names of the Aesir were hereditary titles, whilst others may have been the names of actual people. The following list includes practically all the names of those who belonged to the Aesir at various times: Balder,f Baugi,t Bragi,t Forseti,f Frey,t Heimdal,t Hodur,t Hoeni,t Loki,t Mimir,f Odin,t Thor,t Tyr,f Ullur,! Vali,t Vidar.t Other details are given under Asynjor,t Bifrost,t Dwarfs,t Eddas,! Giants,t Horses,t Nibelungenlied,t Norns,t Ragnarok,! Sumars Blot,t Treasures,t Valkyries,! Volsung,f Volva,! Zodiacal Houses.!
Aesma. The Zoroastrian Deva or evil spirit of wrath, now the modern Khasmf or Khism, to whom was applied the term ‘with the terrible spear.’ He was the inspirer of vengeance and of perseverance in evil; he was third in the hierarchy of demons, his good opponent being Craosa.t In the Book of Tobit he was known as Asmodeus.t
A!. A form of Ra,t the Egyptian sun god, as he nightly journeys through the underworld.
Aflkoman. The Jewish ceremony of hiding the Passover cake
may be equated with Hapit Qementu (Hapi is found). In this case Ilapi was equivalent to Osiris,! and the piece of Passover cake which is hidden in the ritual is Osiris, while the other two pieces, which are not lost, are Isis! and Nephtys.t
Aganju. In Yorubaf myth he was the son of Oduduaf and brother
and husband of Yemaja.t by whom he was the father of Orunjan!
and of sixteen other gods, including Ogun,! Oko,t Olokun,! Shango,! Shankpanna,! the sun, the moon, and several of the river goddesses.
Agassou. Panther fetish! god of the royal house of Dahomey.!
3



Ahura
Agni
Agni. A fire god of the primitive Aryans who became the divine fire and the spirit of Somat of the Vedic religion. Agni was one of the eight Vasus.t With the development of Brahminism he was one of a triad of gods, the others being Indra/f ruler of the air, and Surya,t ruler of the sky, while he was the ruler of the earth. The numerous references to him in Vedic law show the great importance which was attached to fire by the Indo-Germanic tribes. Horses were sacrificed to him annually, presumably to provide new steeds for his chariot. Matarisvan,t the messenger of the gods, brought Agni to Bhrigu,t the Rishi.f The word ‘ agni’ comes from a Sanskrit word meaning ‘fire,’ and as such has passed into the Slavonic languages.
Ahi. In Hindu myth, an Indian serpent god slain by Trita,t later replaced by Indra,t the war god, of whom it is said: ‘With his vast destroying thunderbolt, Indra struck the darkling mutilated Vritra't (the alternative name for Ahi). In the Vishnu Purana mention is made of a certain Alii Naga,t one of the royal family of Ajudha. In Cambodia the name Naga is generally applied to the seven-headed serpent. In India it means the cobra, with which the ruling families were often connected. Ahi was the son of Danu.f
Ahriman. In Zoroastrian myth the leader of the powers of evil, who is in constant conflict with Ahura Mazda,t the leader of the powers of good. The Devas.t or followers of Ahriman, may well have been the culture heroes and gods of the early Persians before their conquest by the Indo-Germans, and the process of Dualismf may have been designed to offset their attractions for the masses by the increased value given to their opponents, the new gods. It is of interest to note that the name Ahriman was treated in a feminine form in old Persian, which may involve a link with an early mother goddess.
Ahsonnutli. Bisexual god of the Navajot Indians, also known as ‘the Turquoise Hermaphrodite.’ It is possible that he may have
been a later development of the Turquoise Woman, who is frequently
mentioned in Navajo myth. He is considered as the creator of heaven and earth and to have placed men at each of the cardinal points to uphold the sky.
Ahura. In the early Aryan languages there were two general
words for God: Asurat (Zend, Ahura) and Daiva (Sanscrit, Deva,t Zend, Daeva). In the Vedast Deva meant a benign power and
Asura an evil power. To the Zoroastrians Daeva meant an evil
power and Ahura meant a benign one. In Buddhismt both words
had largely lost their meaning.
Ahura Mazda. In the Zoroastrian theogony the chief benign divinity as opposed to Anra Mainyut or Ahriman,t leader of the 4



Ahy Aka-Kanet
powers of evil. The name, which means lord of knowledge, can be assimilated to the Vedic Asura.f Coghp, a fifth-century Armenian historian, tells that Hormazut (or Ahura Mazda) and Ahrimanf were
the twin sons of Zervan Akarana,t one of whom was to be the-creator of the earth and of all tilings good. Just before their birth Zervan
said: ‘He who first comes to me, will I make king.’ Hearing this
Ahriman immediately emerged from his mother’s womb and claimed the throne. Zervan, in order not to break his word, gave him the right to rule for 900 years, after which Hormazut would become supreme ruler. He was also the first of the Ameshas.t
Ahy. In Egyptian myth an alternative name for Herusmatauy,t the son of Horust and Hathor.t
Ailill. In Celtic myth an early king of Connaught whose wife Medbt had formerly been the wife of Conchobar.f The name seems to be that of a dwarft or elf,t which presumably means that he was not of Celtic stock. When Fergus! of Ulster had been defeated by Conchobar he sought refuge at the court of Ailill and was well received.
Aimon Kondi. In the Creation Legend of the Arawaksf of Guiana he scoured the world with fire from which Marerewanat and his followers sought refuge in underground caverns. Later there was a great flood from which the survivors escaped in canoes.
Aino. Heroine of the Kalevalat (the Finnish national epic), sister of Joukahainen,t who is pledged to Vainamoinen.t
Aion. Child of Kolpiat and Baauf in the Phoenician Creation Legend!; with Protogonos,! the parent of Genost and Genea; first to discover edible fruits. The word Aion means life.
Air. (a) In the Phoenician Creation Legendt of Damascius, son of Omiclet by Potos,t representing purity without intelligence. He mated with his sister Aura,! and produced Otos,t meaning reason. (b) In the Phoenician Creation Legendt of Philo Byblos, Air and Chaost were in the beginning, and produced Windt (Kolpiat) and Desire! (Potost). (c) In the Phoenician Creation Legend! of Mochus, Air and Ether! engendered Oulomos.t
Airavata. In Hindu myth the Elephant of Indra.t An alternative spelling is Airabata. It was one of the objects produced by the churning of the ocean! in the Kurmat avatar.
Airmid. In Celtic myth the daughter of Diancecht,! the god of healing and sister of Miach,! whom she assisted in the healing of Nuda.t
Aizen Myo-O. Japanese! god of love.
Aka-Kanet. A grain and fruit god of the Araucanian Indians.!
He presides over harvest festivals and appears to have originated as a culture hero. His evil counterpart and possibly twin brother was Guecubu.t

Akatauire Alfatin
Akatauire. In the Mangaiat Island myth he was the brother of Rangi/fthe husband of Ruangeytand one of the co-rulers of the island.
Akra. In Persian myth alternative name for Sinurqh,! the bird of immortality. This word may be related to the Akarana of Zervan Akarana/t the Zoroastrianf Father Time. Akua. In Polynesian myth a generic name for the full gods of Hawaii. Akupcra. In Vedic myth the tortoise on which the earth rests. It has some resemblance to the Kujatat of the Mohammedans. Alaghom Naum. Wife of Patol,! chief deity of the Tzental tribe of the Mayas .t She was known as ‘the Mother of Mind’ and was credited with the creation of mind and thought. She appears to have been a mother goddess whose position was too firm for her to be displaced by her husband. She was also known as Iztat lx.!
Alatuir. The magic stone which in Slavonic! myth was to be found on the island of Bouyan.t From beneath it flowed a river whose waters healed all ailments. At some stage of Slavonic myth Alatuir became a stone at the cross-roads which warned heroes of impending danger, and later still it became the stone on which stood the Cross. Alberich. In the Nibelungenliedt and the Ring Cyclet the dwarft Andvari,! the guardian of the treasures,! is known by this name. As he was also Aelf-Ric the elf king, this presupposes that the position of guardian was hereditary, and that Andvari may have been the family name.
Albiorix. A name given on an inscription at Avignon to a Celtic war god, who may have been Tiwaz.t
Aleion Baal. In Ugarit myth the son of Baal.! He represents an intermediary stage between men and gods, in that in his father’s age-long fight with Mot! both adversaries are killed, only to be resurrected to continue the fight. Aleion is god of the clouds, winds, and rain. He is accompanied by a troop of wild animals, including eight bears. He is god of spring, and possibly that of winter or air. On one occasion he joins with Koser-et-Hasist against his father and is defeated, for which deed he is reproached by Astarte.! Further details are given under Phoenician Creation Legends.
Altar. In Nordic myth there were several groups of dwarfs,! and of these one was named the Alfar. From them comes the word
elf (O.E. czlf) of popular story. A member of the Lovar,! another group of dwarfs, was named Alfr, which may indicate some relationship between these two groups.
Alfatin^ Moorish hero who sleeps with his horse, which is green,
in a cave in the Sierra de Agner from whence he and his steed will 6
4



Alfheim Amalivaca
emerge at the appointed time to avenge Ills people. Green as a colour is connected both with Islam and with fairyland. Other instances are given under Sleeping Princes.t
Alfheim. The home of the elvest or dwarfst in Nordic myth; the dwelling place of Frey/f and also his zodiacal housed Cf. Scott’s Elfhame, in The Rhyme of True Thomas.
Algonquian Indian Creation Legends. One of the myths of this tribe of Red Indians tells how Manibozho,t their culture hero, took refuge on a mountain when a great lake overflowed and submerged the world. He sent out three messengers in succession, a raven, an otter, and finally a musk-rat, before he was satisfied that the flood had disappeared. Another legend tells how Glooskapt and his twin brother Maslumyf representing the good and evil creative powers, formed the solar system and the human race out of their mother’s body. After defeating Maslum, Glooskap was involved in a series of combats with Kewawkqu,t Medecolin,t Pamola,t Wimpe,t over all of whom he was victorious. A third creation legend of this tribe is given under Gros-Ventrest Indian Creation Legend.
Allah. Islamic name for God. Is derived from Semitic El,t and; originally applied to the moon; he seems to have been preceded by Hmaqah,t the moon god. Allatt is the female counterpart of Allah.
Allantide. The apple time, a Cornish name for Samhain.t The word is related to Avalon.t
Allat(u). Female counterpart of Allaht from the pre-Islamicpantheon condemned by the Koran: ‘What think ye then of Al-Lat?’ She is considered to have been the solar goddess Samas.f The Nabateans considered her as the mother of the gods ; she has also been identified with the virgin mother of Petra described by Epiphanius. Allat is also to be equated with Asheratian.t She is occasionally confused with Ninmug,t or Ereshkigel,t queen of thelower world, who ruled over Aralu,t the Babylon Hades, and was the enemy of Ishtar.t She is akin to the Semitic goddess Elat.t Another variant for her name is Ilat.t
Alviss. A dwarft of Nordic myth w'ho is mentioned in the AlvisMai Eddat as having been promised the hand of a daughter of Thort in marriage. When he went to fetch his bride Thor detained him by asking questions until the dawn came and Alviss was forced to depart alone. The story may relate to an early attempt at alliance between the dwarfs and the pre-Aesir.f Amaite-Rangi. In Mangaiat myth he was a sky demon who was
defeated in battle by Ngaru.t
Amalivaca. Culture hero of the Orinocot River Indians wha taught them to till, the soil and instructed them in the arts of life.
7



Amaravati Ament
Amaravati. In Vcdic myth the capital city of Swarga/f the heaven of Indra,f built by Visvakarma.t the architect of the gods.
It is situated on the eastern spur of Mount Meru.t Amasis. In Babylonian myth a name given to Ararat as the landing place of the Ark. May possibly come from the grandson of Noah, who had that name. For further details see Babyloniant Creation Legend. Ama-Terasu. Japanese sun goddess, the child of Izanagit and Izanami,t and sister of Susa-No-0,i the Japanese Poseidon god. From the marriage of these two sprang eight children, from one of whom the Japanese royal family claims descent. The story of her having shut herself up in the Cave of Heaven as a result of her brother’s ill treatment appears to be an attempt to link the personality of the sun priestess with some great catastrophe of the past. She was the most prominent member of the Shinto Pantheon and her exalted rank shows that she belongs to a period anterior to the adoption of Chinese anti-feminist ideas in Japan. She had a sacred bird, the Yatagarasu,t which may be identified with the Yangwut of Chinese myth.
Amathaon. A wizard in Celtic myth who taught his craft to Gwydion,t nephew of Math Hen.t His theft of a dog and a roebuck from Annwnt caused a battle between Gwydion and Arawnt known as the Battle of the Trecs.t He was the son of Danu.t
Amathaounta, or Amathaon. Sea goddess of the Aegean. A part of the tribe of that name founded Hamath in Syria, and another went to Palestine, where it founded Amathus. The Amathites arc mentioned both in Genesis and in Chronicles. Ambika. In Vedic myth an alternative name for Uma,t the wife of Siva,t or of Rudra.t
Ambisagrus. A god of the continental Celts considered by the Romans to resemble Jupiter.
Amemait. In Egyptian myth an animal monster, part crocodile, part lion, part hippo, which devoured the hearts of those who failed to pass the great balance, when weighed by Maatt in the presence of Thoth,t as against the yellow feather (Maat) of virtue, as laid down in the Book of the Dead.t Amemait was also an attendant of the Lord of Amenti,t first region of the Place of Reeds, to whom
were delivered for total destruction the souls and bodies of the unvirtuous.
Amen. The famous cauldron of Ceridwen,t included in the treasures! of Britain.
Ament. In Egyptian myth a mother goddess ; consort of Amon.t
She was probably his predecessor and originated in pre-dynastic times. She is frequently represented with a sheep’s head or with a 8



Amenti Amrita
human head crowned with the diadem of Lower Egypt. At Thebes she was equated with Mut.f
Amenti. In Egyptian myth the Land of the Dead and first region of the Place of Reeds, where dwell souls who lived upon earth offerings. Ruled over by Menuqet.t The name is also given to the West or Other World, as the region of darkness to which souls must go on their way to the Elysian Fields. In the Tuat,t or What is in the Next World, reference is made to the Horn of Ament, the utmost boundary of the horizon of Amenti, as being one of the boundaries of the Elysian Fields. Other regions were Sekhet-Aaru,t SekhetHetep,t Sekhet-Tchant.t Also known as Pet.t
Ameretat. One of the six Immortal Holy Ones, the attendants of Ahuramazda.t Ameretat represented immortality, and was the genius of trees and plants.
Amergin. In Celtic myth one of the leaders of the Milesiant conquerors of Ireland and the first to land there after the murder of Ithf by the three kings of the Tuatha de Danann,t who were subsequently defeated in battle by the invaders.
Ameshas. In the Zoroastrian religion, the six Immortal Holy Ones, the attendants of Ahuramazda,t were thus named. They have been compared with the Seven Spirits before the throne of the Book of Revelation. They were Ameretat,t Aramaiti,t Asha,t Haurvatat,t Khshathra,t Vohu-manah.t Collectively they were also known as the Amshaspands.f
Amida. Japanese Buddhist deity, originally an abstract ideal of boundless light, who is supposed to dwell in the west in some distant retreat. The Daibutsu, some fifty feet high, known as the Great Buddha of Kamakura, is a representation of him.
Amirini. In Yorubat myth an early goddess.
Amon. A pre-dynastic Egyptian god, so ancient that he is never found alone but always in association with some other god such as Raff As Amon-Ra he was ‘ king of the gods, creator of the universe, lord of Karnak, and father of the Pharaohs.’ He was Great God of Thebes, and became Supreme Deity of Egypt during the eighteenth dynasty. His theophany was a ram. The Triad of Thebes was composed of Amon-Ra, his wife Mut,t and his son Khensu.t Originally he appears to have been a god of agriculture, and as such became associated with death. At Siwa he was worshipped under the name of Jupiter Ammon.t
Amor. God of the Amorites ; also known as Martu,t husband of Ashirat.t
Amrita. In Vedic myth the ambrosia of the gods. This nectar, which is referred to in the Churning of the Ocean,t is probably
c9

another version of Soma.t The Amrita was stolen by Garudat from Vishnut and only returned after a great struggle. Amset. In Egyptian myth one of the four divine sons of Horus,t

A name by which the Ameshasyf the six Immortal Holy Ones, attendants of Ahuramazda,t were also known. Amsu. In Egyptian myth alternative name for Min.t An. In Egyptian myth a title meaning sun (or moon) god, given to Osirist in the Lament of Isist and Nephtys.t
Anahita. In Zoroastrian myth a river goddess, at some stage associated with Mithra.t She is the mother goddess of Armenia, may be equated with Anthat.t She was one of the Yazatas.t
Anaitis. In Ugarit myth an alternative name for Anthat.t
Ananta. In Vedic myth a name given to Sesha,t the chief of the Nagas,t and ruler of Patala.t
Anat. In Ugarit myth the daughter of Ashtart.t Alternative name for Atargatis.t Anata. In Babylonian myth the wife of Anu,t and mother of Enlii.t
Anathva. In the Creation Legend of the Karayat Indians of eastern Brazil the deluge was caused by a malevolent being of this name. The ancestors of the tribe escaped, thanks to Saracura,t the water hen, bringing earth to the hill-top on which they had sought refuge as fast as the fish, sent by Anatiwa, nibbled it away. This legend is shared with the Gest Indians and seems to be mainly concerned with them as in another version of the story the ancestors of the Karaya sought refuge in a cavern of safety from which they were brought to the surface by Kaboi.t The name given to the hilltop was Tupimare.t
Ancestor Worship. The system of ancestor worship practised by the Chinese, and in other parts of the world, is an essential step in the process of creating gods from cultural heroes.t The rank of god was originally a posthumous one; the assumption of divinity by royalty and emperors on their coronation seems to be a later stage of development. For further details see Fravashis.t
Andaman Islands Myth. See Pulug,t the thunder god.
Andjeti. Early human-headed form of Osiris,t the Egyptian god, in the Nile Delta.
Andvari. In Nordic myth he was the guardian of the treasures! io

Anet. In Egyptian myth one of the pair of sacred fish which swam before the boat of Raf to warn him of danger. It also announced the rise of the Nile. The other was Abtu.t
Angarua. Wife of Mokoiro,t co-ruler of Mangaiat Island.
Angiras. In Vedic myth one of the seven great Rishist or Prajapatis.t He was the reputed author of some of the Vedic hymns. He was the father of the preceptor of the gods, Vrihaspati.t
Angurbodi. A Nordic giantess,! wife of Loki,! and mother of Fenrir,! Hela,! and Jormungard.t Angus. In Celtic myth son of Dagdat and Boann.t He was the Mac Oct or the Young God, the Celtic Eros who carried off Etain,tthe wife of Mider.t In the story of Diarmaitt and Grainnet he repeatedly saves them from the vengeance of Finn.! When the Tuatha de Danaant were defeated by the Milesians! and were allotting Sidhe,! or hill forts, to those of their members who remained in Ireland, Angus was forgotten. He eventually managed to displace his father Dagda from his palace at Brugh on the Boyne.
Anhur. Human-headed Egyptian war god and sun god of Abydos. At Sebennytus he formed a dual god with Shu,! and was regarded as son of Hathor.t He was also known as Onouris.!
Anit. Egyptian goddess equated with Hathor.t and wife of Mentuf and mother of one of the Horust gods.
Ankh. Egyptian symbol of life carried by gods and royalty, usually held by the upper loop. For further details see Shen.t
Anna-Purna. In Vedic myth an aspect of Parvati,! wife of Siva.! The name means ‘Full of Food,’ and it is for her capacity for providing this that she is worshipped. She is depicted seated on a water-lily, holding a dish of rice in one hand and a spoon in the other.
Annwn. In Celtic myth the underworld ruled over by Arawn.t It was from here that one of the magic cauldrons,! with a ridge of pearls round the brim and tended by nine maidens, was acquired by the Celts.

Anquet. Egyptian goddess, third of the Elephantine Triad,! called the Lady of Sati, where she had a temple. She was associated with the fertilizing waters of the Nile. She was also goddess of II

Anti-Indian Creation Legends. The Creation Legends of the Ipurinas and Yurukares of Bolivia and north-western Brazil have a myth that at the time of some great catastrophe human beings sought refuge in a great cave from which they emerged with great difficulty after the deluge, which succeeded the great conflagration, as the entrance to the cave was blocked. Those who did not seek refuge in the cave were presumably killed, as when the refugees emerged the only living beings were monkeys. The world was repopulated by Titit and by Ulet and his wife.

Anubis Ao-Potango
Supreme king of heaven, father of the gods, ruler of destiny. One of the most ancient divinities, his name being found on earliest known inscriptions. He was mainly worshipped at Erech and Der.
By Anatat he was father of Enlilt (or Belt). He was also the father
of Gibilt and Nusku.t The name means ‘Expanse of Heaven, ’t and he may have been an early personification of the Heavenly Father. His counterpart in the lower regions was Kingu.t In the Babylonian Creation Legendt of Damascius he was the son of Assorust and Kissare.t
Anubis. Ancient jackal-headed Egyptian deity, presiding over embalming of the dead; was local god of Abydos; child of Osirisf and Nephtys,t and after having been exposed by his mother, was found by Isist with the help of some dogs. He grew up to be her guard and attendant in her wanderings; his name means watcher and guardian of the dogs. With Upuaut,t he presided over the abode of the dead and led them to the judgment hall and supervised the weigliing of the heart. His cult is probably older than that of Osirisf and totemistic in origin. Alternative story makes him the son of Sett; his deification may have been to prevent jackals from devouring the bodies of the dead. Also known as Anpuf or Anupf or Wip.f
Anunaki. Babylonian gods of the earth created by Mardukf in contrast to the Igigif or spirits of heaven. They may be the stars of the northern heavens.
Anunitum. In Babylonian myth a goddess with temple at Sippar. Later merged with Ishtar,f although records of her worship continued until the time of Nabonidus, 555-538 B.c.
Anup. In Egyptian myth an alternative name for Anubis.f
Anzety. An Egyptian god of Busiris, who preceded Osiris,t and was represented by a human head on a pole, with arms wielding the crook and the flail.

Apep. Egyptian snake god, a manifestation of Set,t enemy of Rat and lord of the Powers of Darkness. As enemy of the sun gods, he was also the enemy of the dead, who could only return to life through his defeat. He is depicted as a great serpent and would appear to be a pre-dynastic storm god, an assumption which is confirmed by his title of the Roarer. He was said to have been slain by Rat at the foot of the sacred sycamore-tree of Nutt at Heliopolis. Every morning Apep menaced the rising sun, but by the recitation of a powerful spell devised by Thotht the sun was able to render harmless the efforts of Apep.
Apet. Egyptian hippopotamus goddess of Thebes; a local form of Taueret.t She was also known as Opet.t
Apo. In Zoroastrian myth, one of the Yazatas,t the genius of sweet waters.
Apis. In Egyptian myth the sacred bull in whom Osirist was believed to be incarnate. For further information see Serapis.t
Apisirahts. The morning Venus god of the Blackfoott Indians.
Apocatequil. Chief priest of the moon god of the Incas, the son of Guamansurit and the twin brother of Piguerao.t He was a god of the lightning and statues to him were put up on mountain tops all through the empire of the Incas. After his mother had been treacherously murdered by her brothers, the Guachimines,t he brought her to life again and after having slain them was guided by Ataguchut to make a hole from the Cave of Refuge by which they were to reach the land of the Incas.

Apsaras
Apsaras. In Vedic myth, the celestial nymphs of Swarga,t the heaven of Indra.t They appear to correspond to the hourist of Islam, and the perist of the Persians. They were beautiful fairy
like beings, whose charms were ‘ the common treasure of the host of heaven.’ Associated with them are the Gandharvasyf the heavenly choristers. The greatest of the Apsaras was Rambha.t
Apsu. In Babylonian myth the deep watery abyss from which sprang all things. She was the Akkadian version of Tiamat,t mother of Ea,t and was also known as Zigarunt, ‘The Mother that begat Heaven and Earth.’ Later, with the decay of matriarchal ideas, she changed her sex and became the husband of Tiamatt and the father of Mommu.f A great open bowl called an apsu was placed in the temple courts of the water gods : Ea,t Enki,t Nina,t Ningirsu,t and others. That this is intimately connected with memories of the deluge and with the Tannur of Islam is certain. The Jews, returning from Babylon, placed a sea in the temple but, not understanding its significance, allowed the priests to wash in it (x Kings vii. 23 ff. ; 2 Chron. iv. 6). In the Babylonian Creation Legendf of Damascius he was known as Aphsont and married Tauthe.t
Apuat. In Egyptian myth an alternative spelling for Upuaut.t
Apu-Hau. In Polynesian myth one of the children of Tawhiri,t the god of hurricanes and storms. The name means Fierce Squalls.
Apu-Ko-Hai. A fish god of Kanei who occurs in the myths of Mangaia.t
Apu-Matangi. In Polynesian myth one of the eleven storm gods who were the children or the bodyguard of Tawhiri.t The word means Whirlwind.
Aqas-Xena~Xenas. Hero of a Chinookt story of an Indian boy who reached the upper regions by a chain secured to the end of an arrow ; there he reached the abode of the Evening Star and found his family counting over the dead people in Evening Star’s game-bag. He marries the Moon, the daughter of Evening Star, and participates in a war with the Morning Star and its daughter the Sun. His children are Siamese twins and are eventually successfully divided by Blue-Jay.t This appears to be a vague memory of some great cosmic happening of the remote past.
Aquqim. In Phoenician myth fabulous creations sent by Elt to combat Baalf according to the Ugarit texts. Sometimes
known as Okelim.t For further details see Phoenician! Creation Legends.
Aralu. In Babylonian myth Hades, ruled over by Allatut or Ereshkigel,t queen of the underworld, and by Nergal.f A somewhat depressing reflection of the upper world, those who entered it being 15

Aramaiti
doomed to remain for ever in the Stygian gloom, to live on mud and dust. In the Gilgamesht Epic there would appear to be a superior abode for those slain in battle, who lie on couches and drink pure water. It was customary to provide food and drink for the dead so that they should not have to wander about in search of nourishment. Aramaiti. One of the six Immortal Holy Ones, the attendants of Ahuramazda.t Aramaiti represented modesty and piety, and was the genius of the earth. Sayana, the greatest ancient Indian commentator on the Rig Veda, says the name means ‘ The Earth ’ perhaps it may ha%'e been Ara Mater or Mother Earth, which would make her an early mother goddess.

Araucanian Indians. The gods of this tribe of South American Indians included Aka-Kanet,t Epunamun,! Guecubu,! and Pilan.t
Arawak Creation Legends. The myths of this Guiana tribe tell how Makonaimat created the beasts of the forest and placed his son Sigut over them as king. In the forest was a tree of knowledge which Sigu cut down in order to plant its seeds all over the earth. From the stump, however, water began to gush forth and soon turned into a great deluge. The birds and climbing animals took refuge on the tree tops while the others were led by Sigu into a cave where they remained in safety until the disaster was over. There are other variants of this myth in Guiana. Aimon Kondi,! who may be taken to be the same as Makonaima, first burnt the world with fire from which those who took refuge in caverns were the only ones to escape. They included Marerewanat and his followers. The Macusis believe that the survivors of the deluge turned stones into human beings to populate the earth. In the Tamanac
myth a man and a woman were saved by climbing to the top of a high mountain. The Warrau tribe tell how' Okonorote,t their culture hero, led the tribe to earth and how Korobonaf was seduced by a water demon and gave birth to the first Carib.f Finally Maiso,t the mother goddess of the Paressis, was the parent of all living things. They share a culture hero Kamut with the -Bakairit Caribs, the Karayas,t and the Paraguayans.! Details of this myth are given under Tupaya! and Ges Indian Creation Legends.

Arianrhod. In Celtic myth she was the daughter of Befit and Don,! and the sister of Gwydion! and Amathaont and the mother by Gw'ydion of Lieut and Dylan.! Arthur. An early British culture hero who appears at some time to have acquired the personality and many of the deeds of Gwydion.f That there w'ere several Arthurs is reasonably certain, the last being the military leader mentioned by Geoffrey of Monmouth. The collection of stories known as the Arthurian Legend are to a large extent Christianized versions of early Celtic myth, and, as such, do not fall within the scope of tiffs work.
Artio. Early Celtic priestess of a Bear clan who may have been the consort of Essus.t As a goddess she was worshipped in Switzerland at Beme, a name w-hich means ‘bears.’

Aruru. Babylonian mother goddess who, with Marduk,! created ‘the seed of mankind.’ This is an older tradition to that in which Marduk created mankind from his own blood or from that of Kingu.t which would show that she is pre-diluvial. Zarpanitt (or Zen panitumf) is possibly another form of this. In the Babylonian Creation Legendt Aruru created Enkit from a piece of clay manufactured from her own spittle. Also known as Nintu.t Aryman. In Vedic myth one of the twelve Adityas,! or guardians of the months of the year.

Asari. Originally an agricultural god of Syria, who later became confused with Osiris,! the Egyptian god.
Asartaiti. The ‘Swathed One,’ a title sometimes given to Osirist in Egyptian myth. Aschochimi Indian Creation Legend. This tribe of California Indians tell of a great deluge in which all mankind was drowned. Coyote,t however, created a fresh crop of humans by planting feathers plucked from birds. This type of flood myth usually implies that the survivors of the tribe had to bring in those rescued from other ethnic groups in order that the race might be continued.
Aserab. Wife of Elt and mother of Atari in the Ugarit texts.
Aset. Alternative name for Isist in Egyptian myth.
Asgard. The fabled city of the Aesir,t from which Odint and his followers set forth to battle. It included Valhalla,! the great hall of the castle of Gladsheim.t The Nordic world was divided into Asgard, the home of the Aesir; Jotunnheim,! the home of the giants!; Svartheim,! the home of the dark elves!; and Mannheim,! the home of men. It was connected with the outside world b}r Bifrost,! the rainbow bridge. The word Asgard means the abode of the Ass or Aesir.
Asgaya-Gigagei. Bisexual thunder god of the Cherokee! Indians, known as The Red Man or The Red Woman.
Ash. In Egyptian myth a three-headed deity—lion, snake, and
vulture—of foreign origin. His name occurs only five times in inscriptions from the eleventh to the eighteenth dynasty. In the fifth dynasty Ash is called the God of the Land of Tehennu, or Land of the Olive-tree.
Asha. One of the six Immortal Holy Ones, the attendants of Ahuramazda.t Asha represented righteousness and was genius of
fire. He may have been an early Sre god. He was one of the Yazatas.t 18

Ashirat. Name given to Venus, the evening star, in the Akkadian mythology, where her husband is given as Amor.t In the Ugarit scripts she was a sun goddess. >
Ashtareth. In Babylonian myth an alternative spelling for Ashtart.t
Ashtart. Of the various spellings of the name, Astartet is found in the Tel Amara letters. The Hebrew Ashtoretht arose when the rabbinical school of the Massoretes in the sixth century decided to adopt a conventional system to compensate for the lack of vowels in written Hebrew, and at the same time to insert in the names of foreign divinities the vowels from the word ‘boshet/ meaning abomination. Asherahf is the Ugarit version, while by Lucian she is called Syria Dea. The use of the name Atargatist appears to have arisen from a lack of understanding of Syriac. She was the fertility goddess of the Semitic races, her cult having spread throughout the wrhoIe Middle East. In Babylonia she became Ishtar.t By the Greeks she was equated with Aphrodite,t who would appear to be the same but in a new setting. As the goddess of the planet Venus she would appear to be a variant of Attarf the South Semitic Venus god. As Astoretht she was a goddess of war in Egypt from 1S00 B.c. until the coming of Christianity. She was known as the lady of horses and chariots, and depicted as lioness-headed and mounted on a quadriga, although it is possible that this is a confusion with Anthat.f In the Ugarit texts she is also Asheratiant (the Asheru of the Sea), wife of El,t creatress of the gods, being the mother of seventy gods and goddesses, including Baal,t Anat,t Kathar-Wa-Hasis,t Athar,t the most active of the Ugarit pantheon. In fact she w'ould appear to have been a high priestess of a pre-diluvial fertility cult. She was the Baalatht of Byblos. Also known as Ashtareth,
t
Ashtoreth, Astarte, and Qedeshet.t
Ashtoreth. Hebrew variant of the Babylonian Ashtart.t

Ask
serve as messenger from the great gods to Allatu,t Queen of Hades, to demand the release of Ishtar.f In her rage at this, Allatu cursed him
with a terrible curse condemning him to dwell in darkness and to feed on garbage. Ask. In the Nordic creation mytht he and his wife, Embla,t were the first humans, having been carved out of wood or saved from
a dug-out canoe by Odint and his brothers, the sons of Bor,t who may be presumed to have escaped from the Flood by other means. The word means Ash, and is frequently prefixed to Yggr-drasil,t the tree of life. Asmodeus. Name given in the Book of Tobit to Aesma,t the Zoroastrian spirit of wrath. The term was possibly derived from Aesmadaeva, the furry demon. The Median folk story from which the Book of Tobit is derived shows marked resemblances to the Persian original. In the Talmud, Asmodeus is king of the tribe of demons known as the Shedin. Ass. In Egyptian myth a form of Ra.t Later eaten by a monster serpent.
Asshur. Originally an Assyrian moon and war god of city of that name. Later became head of pantheon, occupying a similar position to that of Marduk.t He may be a later development of Anshar.t His symbol, a god in a horned cap, shooting an arrow from a bow, enclosed in a circle, was the ensign under which the Assyrians marched into battle, and was to be found in one form or another wherever the rule of Assyria spread. His consort was Ishtar.t There is also a dragon myth connected with him.
Assorus. Name given to Anshart by Damascius in the Babylonian Creation Legend.
t
Asfar. Southern Semitic spelling of Ishtar,t as goddess of the evening star.
Astarte. Child of Ouranost and Geat in the Phoenician Creation Legendt of Philo Bybios; and sister of Baitulos,t Dagonyt Pontus,t and Zeus Demaros.t For further information see Ashtart.t
Asura. A name applied in the Vedast to the ruling families of the Nagat civilization, who were defeated by the Hindu invaders. They would appear to have reached a higher degree of culture than their Aryan rivals. The Brahmans attributed to them wealth and luxury, the use of magic, superior architectural skill, and the ability to restore the dead to life. The Asuras included the Daityasyt the Danavas,t the Dasyus,t the Rakshasas,t and Vritrat destroyed by Indra.t The word Asura has the same meaning as Ahura.t
Asvins. In Yedic myth, twin gods the sons of Saranyu.t Originally they were cosmic deities, but later, under their individual names of Dasra and Nasatya, were known as the Divine Physicians. 20



Asynjor Atharvan
They may be taken to have been early Vedic culture heroes, who
were raised to divine rank because of their importance. They may have been the inventors of mead, the honey wine of the ancients, as a drink called ‘madhu’ is associated with their cult.
Asynjor. In Nordic myth the attendants of Freyat or Frigga.f Further details, together with a list of the better known, are given in the Appendix.
Ataguchu. A god of the Incast who was involved with Pigueraot and Apocatequilt in the Inca creation myth in that he instructed them how to get out of Pacari,t the Cave of Refuge, which they had sought during some great disaster.
Atar. In Zoroastrian myth, one of the Yazatas,t the genius of fire. The ninth month, and the ninth day of each month, were named after him.
Atar. According to the Ugarit Tablets the son of El| and Aserah,t known as The Terrible, chosen to rule the world for a space after the killing of Baalf by Mot.f May be equated to Atter,t the male Venus god of war of the northern Semites.
Atar Gatis. In Babylonian myth corrupt form of Atar’ate, itself a contraction of Ashtartf-Anat.t The reference is from Lucian. The meaning is probably Ashtart mother of Anat, or alternatively Atarf father of Anat.
Ataryatis Derketo. Syrian fish goddess of Ascalon, said to be mother of Semiramist by Oannes.f
Atatarho. A culture hero of the Iroquoist who was always attired in a garment of living snakes symbolical of his power as a warrior and as a magician.
Aten. In Egyptian myth the god of the solar disk who was monotheistically worshipped by Akhenaten, a religious theory originating in Heliopolis, which only lasted for one reign. This cult may have been more in the nature of a political move against the priesthood of Amonf in other towns.
Athapascan Indian Creation Legend. This tribe of Indians of north-west America have a myth that after the deluge Yetl,t the Great Raven, dragged the earth from the waters and became the ancestor of the tribe and taught the first humans the use of fire.
Athar. i. Child of Asheratianf in the Ugarit pantheon. 2. At the time of the abandonment of the matriarchy and the degradation of women, Ishtarf became a male god in southern Arabia under this name.
Atharvan. In Vedic myth one of the seven great Rishist or Prajapatis.f He was the reputed author of the fourth Veda, and was said to be the first to open the ways by sacrifice. 21



Athensic Audhumla
Athensic. In the myths of the Iroquoist tribes the ancestress of mankind who fell from heaven into the waters of the deluge as it was receding, and later she found herself on dry land, which became a continent.
Athyr. In Egyptian myth an alternative spelling for Hathor.t
Atius-Tirawa. Chief deity of the Pawnees,! who figures in their Creation Legend and who endeavoured to destroy the world by fire,
the conflagration being put out by the deluge. He was a prediluvial culture hero with a knowledge of astronomy as he was supposed to have ordered the movement of the stars and planets. He is sometimes referred to as Tirawa.f

A Teutonic word meaning grandfather occasion
ally applied to Thor.t In the Volsung Sagat he was a king who married Gudrunt and was subsequently slain by her. Called Etzel in the Nibelungenlied.
Atmu. In Egyptian myth a local deity of Heliopolis. Subsequently became merged with Rat as Ra-Tem.t At a later date the name Atmu was given to the setting sun. The name is sometimes spelt Atum.t Atmu was a member of the Ennead.t He was the father of Shut and Tefnut,t and as such dated back to the early stage of Egyptian religion, and was associated with one of the two Deluge Legends of Egypt, the other being given under Ra. Atmu is said to have let loose the waters of the Great Deep over the earth, only those who were in his boat escaped with their lives. In view of the similarity of the name to Tamtu,t the Babylonian name for the Bitter Sea-waters, it seems as if we have here a memory of the Deluge, linking the myths of the Nile Valley with those of the Fertile Crescent. For further details see Egyptian! Creation Legends.
Atri. In Vedic myth one of the seven great Rishis.t
