Ear. One of a series of names given in south Germany to Tiwaz or Tyr.t Easter Island Creation Legend. The earliest myths of Easter Island refer to struggle for supremacy between the ‘Big Ears’ and the ‘Small Ears.’ It is not now possible to say where the ‘ Big Ear’ people, the prototype of the statues, came from; but there are elements of Melanesian culture which appear to be similar. The colossal stone statues of long-eared people wearing pumice-stone hats have long been a mystery to archaeologists; they possibly represented a link with some vanished culture of the Pacific. Unfortunately it has not been possible to decipher the petroglyphs or to interpret the drawings of birds and birdmen, which seem to relate to some unusual form of worship. The native name for Easter Island is Rapanui.t They had a creator god, Meke Meke,t who may have been Tangaroa,t the Polynesiant god.
Ebisu. Japanese god of labour, the son of Daikoku.t He is usually depicted with a fishing rod, and a fish under his arm, and may originally have been a god of the fishermen’s clan. He was one of the seven divinities of luck, the Shichi Fukujin.t
Eda Male. Twin idols, male and female, used by the Ogoboni tribe of the Yorubast in their initiation ceremonies. 64



Eddas
Eddas
Eddas. The Eddas, the source books of Nordic myths, have come
to us mainly in three texts. The oldest of these is the Elder Edda or Codex Regius, considered to have been drawn up by the Icelandic historian Saemund about the year 1090. The next is_a Christianized version by Saxo Grammaticus written in 1190 and included in the Gesta Danorum. Unfortunately this version has been so modified in accordance with the existing religious sentiments as to be almost valueless. The third and last version is the Prose Edda drawn up by Snorri Sturlason in 1220. The vast majority of the stories and myths of the Scandinavians have come to us from the Elder and the Prose Eddas. The fact that both these codices were discovered in Iceland seems to prove that the disintegrating impact of Christianity only reached that country after the legends had been reduced to writing, and was therefore too late to be fully effective. The stories of the Eddas are independent of the great body of Celtic myth, although there are certain points, particularly in the treasures,! where they tend to overlap. They bear the impression of the early struggles of at least four racial groups involved in the development of the Northlands. These were the giants,! whom we take to be the first hyperborean inhabitants (the Frost Giants! may have been the heroes of the Kalevalaf and the Hero of Estonia!), and the dwarfs,! who were eventually dominated by a combination of two invading groups, the Vanirt and the Aesir.t In a similar manner to the Celts the Norsemen appear to have been incapable of working metals and were forced to rely upon the services of the dwarfs,! who may be considered to be a tribe of short, sturdy, Central European stock specializing in metal working and other crafts. An alphabetical list of the works included in the Elder and the Prose Eddas is given below. Details about them will be found under the appropriate headings. Aegisdrekka or Lokasenna (Aegir’sf Carousal or the Taunting of Lokit) ; Alvis-Mal (The Lay of Alvisst) ; * Braga Raedur (The Conversations of Bragif); *Eptirmali; Fjol Svinns Mai!; * Formali
;
Grimnis-Mal (The Lay of Grimnirf) ; The Magic Lay of Groa-Galdur;
* Gylfa-Ginning (The Deluding of Gylfi!) ; Harbards-Ljod (Lay of Ilarbardt); * Hattatalt; Hava-Malf ; Hrafna’Galdurt Odins (Odin’s Raven Spell); Hymiskvida (Lay of Hymirf); Hyndlu-Ljod (The Chant of Ilyndla!) ; Rigs-Thula (The Lay of Rig!) ; * Skaldska Parmalt; Skimis-For (Skimir’s Quest!); Solar-Ljodt; Thryms-Kvida (The Lay of Thrymf); Vafthrudnis-Kvida, Vafthrudnismal (The Lay of Vafthrudnit); Vegtams-Kvida (The Lay of Vegtamt); Volsungt Cycle; Volundar-Kvida (The Lay of Volundt); Voluspa.t The Chant of Ilyndla! and the shorter version of the Voluspat are in the Flatey.Book.t The Yngling Saga! and the Yngling Atal! are not included in the Eddas. Efrit. The second most powerful dass of jinnt in pre-Islamic myth. Egg. In the Phoenidan Creation Legendt of Philo Byblos, Mot,t the primeval egg, was the child of Wind! and Desiret (Potos). In the Phoenician Creation Legendt of Mochus, the egg was produced by Oulomosf and when broken gave rise to Ouranost and Gea.t The Ghaddarf and certain other jinnt are said to have sprung from an egg: this may be a distorted Arabian version of the Creation Legends. See also Narayana,t Ghaddar/f Egyptian Creation Legends, Khnemu.t Egih In Nordic myth the brother of Volund,t who at the court of King Nithudt shot with an arrow an apple o2 the head of a young boy, a feat later attributed to William Tell. Later he assisted Iris brother to escape. He was the son of Badi.t
Egyptian Creation Legends. There is a great similarity between the Creation Legends of the Nile Valley and those of the Fertile Crescent. In all of them the beginning was a primitive chaotic mass of waters known to the Babylonians as Tamtu,t Tiamat,t or Tiawath,t and to the Hebrews as Tohut or Tchom.t One would therefore expect the Egyptian chaos monster to have had a name fitting into this common pattern, but instead he is called Nun,t or Nu.t However, there is also a primeval god called Atmu,t who is said to have caused the Deluge, from which it would appear that he or she was the original mass of waters, and that at some later date Nun or Nu was installed in this position. There are several alternative versions of the actual creation process, of which the simplest is that the sun arose from the waters, and that when the light had made a rift between the earth and the sky, four pillars, marking the cardinal points, were erected to hold up the heavens. At Hermopolis, however, Thotht was the mind and intelligence of Nu, and, with the aid of Khnemut and Ptah,t created several pairs of gods, including Herat and Hehut, Kekuit and Kekuit, Qeh and Qerhit, and afterwards the remainder of the universe. At Sais, Neith,t the self-created virgin goddess, became the mother of Ra,t from whom sprang the gods. At Hermopolis, Khepera,t the scarab beetle god, created Gebt and Nut,t the parents of Osirist and Isist and of other gods. At Elephantine it was Khnemuyf the potter god, who created the universe, made the cosmic eggt from the mud of the Nile, and shaped man on his potter’s wheel, while at Memphis the same part was played by Ptah,t who in the process of development seems to have absorbed Tenen,t an early creator god. Contrary to general opinion, there are at least two Egyptian Deluge legends, of which the earliest says that the god Atmu caused the waters of the great deep to overflow and drown every66



Egyptian Egyptian
body, except those who were with him in his boat. This would
indicate that when Atmu, as mentioned above, was deposed frorn the post of god of the mass of waters, he, in turn, took over the functions
of the Egyptian Noah or Uta-Napishtim,t whose name may some day
be discovered.
The other Deluge legend tells how Ra, being offended with his subjects, ordered Hathorf and Sekhmet,t the fire goddess, to destroy
them all. After they had partially completed their work, and were
wading in human blood, Ra relented, and, being unable to persuade them to desist, flooded the world with beer, which the goddesses drank to such an extent that they forgot all about their dreadful mission. This rewriting of an early myth of volcanic catastrophe followed by flood is rather different to those of the countries to the
north-east, where there are references mainly to floods. There may also be a faint recollection of a flood in the hieroglyph of the Bennut bird. Also there is Manetho’s story of Thotht setting up the Siriadic Columns,t before the Deluge, in order that the records of the past should not be lost.
Egyptian Religion. It is not possible to give more than the briefest outline of Egyptian religion in the space available. The original basis appears to have been the totems of the pre-dynastic tribes, many of which were female, and later became metamorphosed into goddesses and gods. In the earliest stages these birds, beasts, and fishes were recognized for what they were, but later, with the advent of the polytheism which is the sure sign of religions in decay, they all were endowed with the functions of actual gods. Later came the divine Icings, originally perhaps the object of sacrifice, but later to emerge as supreme rulers. The story of the murder of Osirist is clearly that of the death by sacrifice of a barley king. Such beings as Anit,t Anquet,t Hathor,t Isis,t Maat,t Nephtys,t Ptah,t Set,t and Thotht may have been kings and queens, priests and priestesses, who were deified after their deaths; while the stories of the fights between the gods are obviously memories of intertribal wars, in which Set, Osiris, Horus,t Isis, and Nephtys were the names of leading personalities involved. As it is obvious that no human beings were actually present at the Creation, in whatever manner it may have occurred, the Creation Legendst of the Egyptians, in common with those of other countries, must be made up of memories of some great disaster from which
mankind only emerged with great difficulty, combined with priestly ideas as to what might possibly have occurred in the beginning. 1 he gradual change of Osiris from being the god of a most depressing nether world into the potential saviour of the soul must have
taken a long time, and is intimately linked up with the story of his resurrection by Isis and of his begetting Horus in the interval before 67



Ehecatl El
he became ruler of the Kingdom of the Dead. The somewhat excessive preoccupation of the Egyptian with the problems of the next world must have been fostered by the dry climate which made mummification relatively easy, and also by the fact that the system of regulated agrarian life left long periods for leisure and contemplation. The trend towards monotheism, which came to its zenith with the worship of Aten,t appears always to have been that of the ruling classes, as the vast bulk of the populace preferred the potential delights of the Osirian paradise to the somewhat impersonal joys of accompanying Rat in his sun boat. The problem of life in the after-world is revealed by the number of parts of the body and personality which had to be considered. They were the Khatf or physical body; the Kat or double; the Bat or soul; the Khut or aura; the Khaibitt or shadow; the Sekhemt or vital force; the Rent or name; the Abt or will, symbolized as a heart; the Hatit or physical heart; the Sahut or spirit body; and the Ikht or final spiritual state. The Egyptians may take credit for having evolved the first Holy Trinity of Father, Mother, and Child (Osiris, Isis, and the Child Horus), and also for conception of the future life directly related to the moral value of conduct on this earth, and, finally, the first abstract monotheism, in the worship of Aten, in 1375 b.c., which
arose at a time when the Hebrews were still struggling in Canaan. For further details see Book of the Dead,t Egyptian Creation Legends,! and references to named goddesses and gods.

Roman emperor, a.d. 218-22, who was worshipped at Emesa as a variant of Baal.t His image was a black aerolith.
Elat. Semitic goddess, wife of Elf and considered to be the same as Asheratian,t the sea goddess. Also akin to Allat.t She would appear to have preceded Ei by some thousands of years, and to have been the original mother goddess of the Semitic tribes.
Eldhrimir (‘Firefrost’). Another name for Odherir,t the magic cauldron of the Aesir,t applied to it on the occasion of the boiling of the flesh of the wild boar, in all probability Saehrimnir.
Elephantine Triad. Composed of Anquet,t Khnemu,t and Sati.t whose shrines were at Yebu (Elephantine), southern frontier city of Ancient Egypt.
Eleven Mighty Helpers. In Babylonian myth they were enrolled by Tiamatt in her fight against Marduk.t They were the Viper, Lakhmut the Shining Snake, the Great Lion, the Ravening Dog, the Scorpion Man, the Storm Winds, the Fish Man, the Goat Fish. They were armed with the Thunderbolt, the invincible weapon, and commanded by Kingu.t

Elivagar. In Nordic myth the frozen river of Hvergelmir,t the source of twelve rivers called Elivagar from which came the drops

In Celtic myth the city of Dinas Emrys, a name given to Myrddint or Merlin.t It has been identified as a fortification called Broich y Ddinas on the summit of Penmaen. Emrys is also a

Endil. A marine deity of the Celts who may be the same as Dylan.t His name still exists as St. Endellion of the parish of the same name in north Cornwall.
Enigorio and Enigohatgea. In the Iroquoist Creation Legend these were the twin brothers, Enigorio having been the benign creator of rivers, fertile plans, and fruitful trees, and his brother Enigohatgea endeavouring to neutralize all these activities by the creation of deserts, harmful plants, and natural catastrophes. They are the equivalent of Ioskehat and Tawiscarat in the Huron! Creation Legends.
Enki. Babylonian god of fresh water and water god of Eridu
Abu Shahrein. His temple was known as E-Abgu, the House of the Nether Sea. His cult was intimately connected with that of Nina,! Queen of the Waters, who was his daughter, and with that of Ningirsu,! god of irrigation. He was sometimes known as Ea.t He
was said to have been created by Arurut from her own spittle mixed with clay.

Enlightener Epact
Enlightener of the Darkness. In Chineset myth we are told that this dragont produces light by opening his eyes and causes darkness by shutting them. His serpent-like body is a thousand miles long
and of a filthy colour. He never rests and his breath causes wind and cold weather. He can assume nine colours and his breath descends as a rain of water or fire. Gold is the congealed breath of white dragons, crystal the spittle of purple dragons, and glass solidified dragon breath.
En-lil. Originally titular deity of Nippur in Babylonia. He was addressed as ‘the Great Mountain’ and ‘Lord of the Storm,’ from which it is concluded that his original habitat was a mountain-top city ; as there are no mountains in the Euphrates valley, he must
have been the god of a people living in a mountainous area who later emigrated to Babylonia. As Nippur was originally a Sumerian city, it seems safe to presume that the cult of En-lil was of Sumerian origin. The term Lil also means demon; he may therefore have been ‘the Lord Demon’ (see Lilith). In the Great Babylonian Triad of Anu,t En-lil, and Ea,t En-lil was god of the earth. The great Assyrian winged bulls are considered to represent him. He was frequently referred to as Bel,t but the Bel of the Apocrypha was most likely the god Marduk,t who was also referred to by this title. In Assyria, Tiglath refers to En-lil as 'Old Bel,’ and Sargon calls him ‘the Great Mountain.’ En-lil was subsequently displaced by Marduk. He was the father of Sint and Enurta,t and of the heavenly bodies, the sun, the moon, Venus, and, later, Mars. He was son of Anu.t His wife, in some versions, was Ninlil.t
Enmesliarra. Babylonian god of the underworld Aralu,t or Mcslam.t Ministered to by a special kind of priest called Kalu.t
Ennead. Name given to the ‘Paut’ or great company of nine Egyptian gods of Heliopolis: Atmuyf Shu,t Tefnut,t Geb,t Nut,f Osirist and Isis,t Sett and Nephtys.t To this list Rat, Horus/t Uadjit,t IIu,t Saa,t and Khenti Amentit were sometimes added.
Enurta. The name of the Assyrian god of war Ninib,t represented by a bird of prey, is sometimes spelt Enurta. He was the son of Enlil,t and the husband of Gulat and Ba’u.t
Enzu. Alternative name for Nannaryf the Babylonian moon god.
He may be equated with Sin.t
Eostre. Teutonic goddess who gave her name to Easter. The
word may have evolved from Ishtar,t as her festival has some
resemblance to the Babylonian spring festival.

Etan
Etan. In Celtic myth the daughter of Dianchecht/f the Celtic god of medicine, and wife of Ogmayf the god of literature and mother
of Caipre.t
Etana. Babylonian king of the earliest Kish dynasty who subsequently became a god. He became merged with Etana, the hero of an early myth, who was taken to heaven on the back of an
eagle to obtain a herb needed for the safe birth of his son. Etana reached the dwelling of Anu,t but failed to reach that of Ishtar,t which was higher, and fell to earth. The end of the story is lost, but Etana appears to have survived, as he was mentioned in the Gilgamesht epic.
Eterah. In the Ugarit texts a name given to El,t the moon god, as husband of the sun goddess. Other spellings are Jarah,t Jerahjt and Terah.t Eterah was a Semitic word meaning moon.
Ether. In the Phoenician Creation Legendt of Mochus, ‘Ether’ and Airt are the parents of Oulomos.f
Etain. In Celtic myth the wife of Mider,t who was stolen from him by Angus.t
Ethne. In Celtic myth the daughter of Balorf who, having been warned that he would be killed by his grandson, imprisoned her to prevent her marriage. In spite of his precautions she married MacKinely,t or Cian,t and had a son Lugh,t who ultimately killed his grandfather. She may be taken to be the same person as Arianrhod,t the daughter of Beli.f Comparative pedigreesare given under Lleu.t
Etruscan Religion. Owing to the fact that the mystery of the Etruscan language is still only partially solved, much of their religious background is still unknown. Such details as are available come mainly from classical writers, from monuments, and from mirrors. There were twelve great gods, six of each sex, who were called ‘the Senators of the Gods’ and ‘the Penates of the Thunderer Himself.’ Their chief was Tin.t They were fierce and pitiless deities whose name it was forbidden to utter, but they were not deemed eternal but supposed to rise and fall together. Above the great gods were the ‘Shrouded Gods,’ who ruled both gods and men. In practice, however, the three gods of greatest importance were Tin, Cupra,t and Menrva.t The Thunderbolt-hurling Gods, such as
Summanust and Sethlans,t were subordinate to these. Besides these there were Voltumna,t mother goddess; Feronia,t Horta.t Ilythyia-Leucotheayt Thalna,t and Turan,t who were fertility goddesses; Nortia,t the goddess of fortune; Losna,t the
moon goddess; and Fuflunsyf the Bacchic god. Finally there
were the guardians of Hades, Mantust and Ms wife Mania.

Euwe Ewuo
Euwe Peoples. Tliey had two gods Bot and So,t who was also known as Khebieso.t Evnisien. In Celtic myth the son of Penard’unt by her first marriage, and stepbrother of Bran,t King of Britain, and Branwen.t By spite he caused the hatred between them and Matholwch,t and later, when a reconciliation was on the point of being arranged, he burnt his nephew Gwem,t the son of Branwen and Matholwch, to death, and killed himself by jumping into Llassar’s Cauldront of Rebirth. In another version he received the cauldron from Bran and proceeded with it to Ireland as an emissary. There he allied himself with Matholwch and slew his stepbrother in the ensuing war. The story of the embittered hatred of the displaced heir to the throne, for his stepbrother, the King of Britain, and his stepsister, the Queen of Ireland, has within it a tragic element of historical truth. Ewuo. In Yorubaf myth name given to the taboo against women seeing Oro,t the Nigerian bull-roarer, in use.

God * F.’ Mayant war or sacrifice god similar to the Xipet of the Aztecs. He is frequently represented as successfully attacking Ekchuaht (God ‘M’t), the god of travellers. Fa. The god of destiny of the fetish worshippers of Dahomey .t This god has a special name for each follower, and is consulted through his priests on every occasion of import. The basic assumption is that human destiny is only fixed within certain limits, and that by consulting one’s Fa one can obtain advice as to which course of action is the less likely to have ill effects. It does not seem less effective than similar practices elsewhere. Fafnir. In Nordic myth, as expressed in the Volsung Cycle, the Nibelungenlied,t etc., he was the guardian of the treasure. He was later slain by Sigurdt or Siegfried.t In the Volsung Cycle he is described as a giant, but this would seem to be an error in that the guardians of the treasure were usually dwarfs.
Fallas. In Celtic myth the city of the north, one of the four from which originated the Tuatha de Danann.t From it came the Lia Fail, or Stone of Destiny, which was one of the treasures of the Tuatha, and which may be the stone in the Coronation Chair at Westminster Abbey. The other cities were Finias,t Gorias,t and Murias.t There is some resemblance between this and the story of Bouyan.t Fand. In Celtic myth wife of Manannan,t whom he deserted.
Farbauti. Father of Loki,t the Nordic Merlin.t His wife was Laufcy or Nal, and his other children were Byleifstr and Helblindi.f
Faridu. In Persian myth an early king who overthrew Bevarash,t the last of the Azidahaka dynasty of serpent-kings, and condemned him to be bound to Mount Demavand.
Fathach. In Celtic myth the poet king of the Firbolgs.t The
word may be related to Fathit—Irish equivalent for Druids.t He
was the husband of Nessat and the father of Conchobar.t Fathi. Irish equivalent of the Welsh Druids.t The word may be related to Fathach,t the poet of the Firbolgs.t
Fati (or Fadu). In the Society Islands myth, the moon, the son of Rouat by Taonoui.t
Fays. While most fairy lore belongs to a period considerably later than that covered by this work, their origin appears to be linked with the priestesses of the pre-Celtic matriarchal races. In the majority of early accounts the fays are women only, but there
75



Fca Findrina
are a few accounts of fairy men which are not irreconcilable with the foregoing, and the visitor is able to stay in their realms for quite long periods of time without seeing any men, which would indicate either a clan of priestesses as suggested, or alternatively that the men of the tribe were away hunting and would only return for the spring or the autumn fertility festivals, the approach of which would automatically terminate the stay of any outsider who hoped to survive. That they were pre-Celtic is indicated by their not knowing the use of iron and by their inability to count above five. Whether they are to be linked with the dwarfst or not is not clear, but it is significant that there are few references to women in stories about the dwarfs .t Fea. In Celtic myth a war goddess, subordinate to Morrigut

the name means ‘the Hateful.’ Feather Cloak of Freya. This magic garment, named Valhamr,t which is mentioned in the Nordic stories of Thiassit and of Thrym,t appears to have given the user the power to fly through the air. Unfortunately no further details about it are available.
Fenodyree. A name for a hairy being, or satyr, similar to the dwarfst of Nordic myth and the elves,t brownies,t and cluricanest of the Celts. In actual fact it appears to be a memory of the pre-Celtic and pre-Teutonic inhabitants of western Europe.
Fenrir. The Fenris Wolf of Nordic myth, one of the monsters spawned by Loki.t As it could not be restrained by any force of the Aesir,t they asked the dwarfst to make a suitable cord to fetter it, which they did from ‘ the noise of the footfall of a cat, the beards of women, the roots of stones, the sinews of bears, the breath of fish, and the spittle of birds.’ It was as light as silk but strong enough to hold the monster, although in the act of binding him the hand of Tyrf was bitten off. He remained captive until Ragnarok,t when he killed Odint and was slain by Vidar.t He is to some extent linked with Vlkodlaks.t
Fergus. An early king of Ulster who fell in love with Nessa,t the mother of Conchobar,t who consented to marry him on condition that he should hand over to her son the sovereignty for a year, at the end of which time his people refused to restore him to the kingship. Later, while in exile he acted as tutor to a nephew of Conchobar named Cuchulainn,t one of the greatest figures of early Irish history.
Feronia. An Etruscan fire and fertility goddess who is sometimes confused with Cupra.t

Finias. In Celtic myth the city of the south and one of the four from which came the Tuatha de Danann.t From here they brought the sword or spear of Nuda,t later one of the treasurest of the Tuatha. The other cities were Falias,t Gorias,t and Murias.t
Finn. In Celtic myth the son of Cumhal or Camulos,t a king of the Tuatha dc Danann.t As a child he was apprenticed to a magician of the same name who was fishing for the salmon of know
ledge in the pool at the source of the Boyne or Boann.f When the salmon was caught Finn burnt his thumb on it and on sucking it to case the pain became possessed of all knowledge. This story resembles that of the magic cauldronf of Annwn,t particularly as the nine maidens who attend such a cauldron are replaced by nine hazeltrees, a mistaken rendering due to the passage of time. The story of his betrothed Grainnet who eloped with Diarmaitt is told under those headings. He was the father of Ossian.t
Finno-Estonian Creation Legends. Details of these, which greatly resemble each other, are given under Hero of Estonia! and Kalevala.t
Finola. In Celtic myth the eldest daughter of Llyr.t
Fintaan. Husband of Cesarat and father of Larat in a Deluge myth given in the Celtic Creation Legend.t
Firbolgs. In Celtic myth the third dynasty of ancient Ireland following those of Nemedt and Partholon.f It is possible that they occupied portions of Ireland simultaneously with the Fomors,t but the tendency of early myth has been to keep them separate. The Fir Domnann and the Gailioin appear to have been subdivisions of the same tribe, and their first leader was Simeon Brec, known as the Freckled, and the last king was Eochaidh Mac Eire, whose wife Tailitiu became the foster-mother of Lught after their defeat by the Tuatha de Danann.t One of their kings was Fathach.t
Fire. One of the three children of Genosf and Genea in the Phoenician Creation Legendt of Philo Byblos, the others being Flamct and Light.t From Fire came the giants,t including Hyposouraniost and Ousoos.t

Fjorgyn. In Nordic myth the giant mother of Frigga/f and wife of Odint and wife or mother of Thor.t She was one of the Asynjor.t At some later stage a husband was devised for her with the name Fjorgynn. He corresponds to Perkunas.t
Flame. Brother of Firet and Lightt in the Phoenician Creation Legendt of Philo Byblos.
Flatey Book. A fourteenth-century manuscript, containing, in addition to long lists of Norwegian kings, two of the Eddie poems, the Chant of Hundlat and the shorter version of the Voluspa.f
Folkvangr. In Nordic myth the ‘folk meadow’ or dwelling of Frevaf and also her zodiacal housed
Fomors. In Celtic myth the fourth dynasty of ancient Ireland, succeeding that of the Firbolgst and preceding that of the Tuatha de Danann.t They appear to have developed from the Fir Domnann, who were one of the subdivisions of the Firbolgs. Their goddess or queen was Domnu.t Their leaders were Balor,t Bress,t Elthan,t Indech.t Net,t and Tethra.t Like the Firbolgs they were a maritime race and it is possible that in both cases there were many expeditions to Ireland before a settlement was attempted. They were eventually defeated by the Tuatha at the battle of Mag Tuireadh.t
Formosan Creation Legend. Details of this are given under Awun,t Infoniwoo,t and Peiroun.t
Forseti. In Nordic myth one of the Aesirt and the son of Balderf and Nanna.t He was the dispenser of justice and seems to have been a Frisian deity who became absorbed into Scandinavian culture at some early period. Heligoland was once known as Forsetisland and was formerly sacred to him. Humans are said to have been sacrificed to him there, while the sheep on the island were considered to be his property. Among the Teutons the spelling became Vorsitzer (chairman or president), and as such may have been linked with Dings.t His zodiacal houset was Glitnir, which comes under the sign of Virgo, but one is inclined to believe that this is inappropriate and that either he should come under Libra, and have the house of Noatun assigned to Njord,t or he should not appear in the list at all, having taken the place of some early mother goddess, possibly Jord.t
Fravashis. In Zoroastrian myth the guardian ancestor spirits of the believers. The)’ date back to the very earliest periods and are specifically omitted from the Dathas, or fundamental statements of doctrine in the Zend-Avesta.f They constitute a form of ancestor worshipt and are of interest as they show a stage in the process whereby the great ones of the past acquire divine or semi-divine rank. 7S



Frey Fre7a
Frey. In Nordic myth the son of Njordt and Skadi,t who resided
in Alfheim,! which was also tlie name of his zodiacal house,t from
which it may be presumed that he was related to the dwarfs.t He
had a ship Skidbladnir,! built by the dwarfs, which could contain all
the Aesir,t but which could be folded up. His sword, which rendered him invincible, was given by him to Skimirf in return for his services
in persuading Gerda,! a giantess,! to marry him. Owing to this he
fell to the arms of Surtt at Ragnarok.f Adam of Bremen identifies him with Frikka,t the husband of
Frigga,t which is quite possible, although it is not clear whether this might not make him a Teutonic rather than a northern character. He is found associated with the boar, two of which, Gullinbursti and
Slidrurgtanni, drew his chariot, from which it may be assumed that he was originally the chief of a boar clan of the Vanirt who later became merged with the Aesir. Later he became one of the three gods who shared the great temple at Upsala. He is always known
as the brother of Frevat and his promotion to the rank of god may be yet another case of the supersession of a goddess by a god occurring after the end of the matriarchy. The attempts to fuse the personalities of Frey and Yngvi, or Ingr, the chief of the Ingaevones, as put forward in the Ynglinga Saga, do not appear to have any solid foundation. He was called Frea by the Anglo-Saxons.
Freya. In Nordic myth one of the Asynjorf and wife of Odint and mother of Hnossa.t She appears to have been originally a moon goddess and to have travelled in a chariot drawn by two cats, and also to have been priestess of a clan having a hawk totem. This element of confusion may have arisen from the fact that by the time she was taken over by the Aesirt her cult was so ancient that it had already acquired certain portions of other cults. She lived in Folkvangr,t which was also her zodiacal house,! and received half the dead slain in battle. Her marriage to Odin was a purely mythological one. Her personality tended to become merged with
that of Frigga.t who may well have been a different entity. In the later stages of Scandinavian religion she was the goddess of love, of marriage, and of fertility. Her necklace Brisingamen! and her feather cloak,! Valhamr, are included in the treasures! of the Aesir.
On two occasions the giants! tried to get her away from the Aesir; on
the first she was demanded as payment for the building of the wall of Asgardt and was saved by Loki,! and on the second her hand in
marriage was asked for by Thrym,! and on this occasion she was
saved by Loki and Thor.! The word Freya means lad}- in the same
way as Frey means lord. Freya was also known as Gef(jo)nt, Horn,! Mardoll,! Mengladf
or Mcnglod, Syr,t and Vanadis.! Her partial supersession by Frey
was probably a stage in the replacing of the early fertility goddesses
79



Frigga Fujin
by gods. From this goddess we get our Friday, but it is not sure whether the placing of the day in the same position in the week as dies Veneris, or vendredi, is not a coincidence. It seems probable that apart from being representative of one of the mother goddesses she was also a sibyl. The story of how she saved the Vinili from their opponents is told by Paulus Diaconus. Odinf had promised victor)' to the Vandals, the enemies of the Vinili. They appealed for help to Freya, who told them to bring their wives to see Odin early the next morning and for the women to drape their hair round their faces like beards. Odin on seeing these said: ‘Who are
these Langobards [or longbeards] ?
’ Freya said: ‘As you have named them you must give them the victory.’ And this Odin was obliged to do. In the story of Hyndla,t Freya mounts a boar to ride to Valhalla. As, however, it was Freyt whose chariot was drawn by two boars, there must have been some confusion between the two accounts. It seems probable that the boars originally belonged to Freya and were later transferred to Frey.
Frigga. In Nordic myth Frigga appears to have usurped the place of Freyat and to have become not only the wife of Odin! but also the chief of the Asynjor.t She was a Teutonic mother goddess, and while she may originally have been the same as Freya, by the time she was brought into contact with the Aesirt she presented a strong element of competition. The later date of her arrival is shown by the fact that although she had a residence called Fensalir, ‘the Halls of the Sea,’ this never became her zodiacal house,! as was the case with Folkvangr,t the residence of Freya. By Odinf she was the mother of Balder.f
Frikka. Name meaning lover or wooer given by Adam of Bremen to Frey.f
Frimla. A Nordic virgin goddess who wore gold ribbon in her hair. At some period she was absorbed into the Asynjorf as a personal attendant of Frigga.f She may be the same as Fimila.f
Frost Giants. See Giants! and Kalevala.f

Fukurokuju. Japanese god of wisdom and longevity depicted
with a long head and attended by a crane, a deer, or a tortoise. He is one of the seven divinities of luck, the Shichi Fukujin.f
Fulla. In Nordic myth the sister of Friggat and custodian of her magic casket and also her slippers. She was one of the Asynjorf and appears to have been an early mother goddess whose original functions had been partially forgotten. After the death of Nannat she came into possession of her magic ring.
Futsunushi. Japanese fire or lightning god. He was one of the emissaries sent by Ama-Terasuf to force the abdication of Onamujif in order that Ninigit could come to the throne. His associate in this work was Takemikadzuchi,t the thunder god.

G
God ‘G.* The sun god of the Mayas,t who is in all probability Kinich-Ahau.t He can be recognized by the sun sign Kin and his filed front teeth.
Gaea. Alternative spelling for Gea.t
Galar. In Nordic myth one of the dwarfs!—the other being
Fjalarf—who murdered Kvasirf and the giantt Gilling.t
Gandharvas. In Vedic myth the heavenly choristers of Swarga,t the heaven of Indra.f They are usually found associated with the Apsaras,t the celestial nymphs of Swarga. They were said to have a great partiality for women, and the word Gandharva was used to describe a marrage ‘from affection without any nuptial rite.’
Ganesa. In Vedic myth the god of wisdom and the patron of literature. He is usually depicted with the head of an elephant. The story goes that his mother, Parvati,t showed him to Siva,t whose glance destroyed his head, which was replaced by that of an elephant. As Ganesa is corpulent, it is possible that his designation as an elephant may have been due to his bulk and to his rank as the leader of the armed forces of Siva, in which case the elephant’s trunk would be a later addition. In Japan he was known as Shodent. In Swargat his celestial city was Kailasat on Mount Meru.t
Ganga (the Ganges River). In Vedic myth the Ganges was the most sacred of all the rivers in India. It was formerly the custom for pilgrims to start from the source of the river at Gangotri and to walk down the left bank to the mouth at Ganga-sagara, and then to return to the source by the right bank. This pilgrimage, which takes six years to accomplish, is called Pradakshini, and great merit was acquired by it. The guide to persons undertaking these pilgrimages was known as a Gangaputra. The sacredness of this river is due to the belief that it flows from the toe of Vishnu.t Its waters are said to hold great curative properties.
Gann. In Nordic myth the hell-dog slain at Ragnarokt by Tyrt in a terrible conflict in which both participants died. It is the same as the moon hound of Teutonic myth, and the white dogs with red ears of Arawn.t which are included in the treasures! of Britain.

Ghaddar. In pre-Islamic myth jinnt found in the Yemen and in Upper Egypt, who enticed men and tortured them, or just terrified them, and then left them. They are said to be the offspring of Iblist and his wife ; they came from an egg which may have been the cosmic egg.t

Ghul. In pre-Islamic myth female jinnt opposed to travel. The male of the species is the Qutrub.t From Ghul comes the modem word ghoul. They were cannibals, and often appeared to men in the desert, and, occasionally, prostituted themselves to them.
Giants. Mythology and fable are full of stories of giants, and it is felt that these may be divided into two groups. The first group, of which the Jotunnt are typical representatives, is composed of people who are slightly taller and larger than the tellers of the stories, in the manner that a Scandinavian may seem a giant to an Italian. These differences are merely of a racial character and do not necessitate more than the interminglings of peoples at the time of the great migrations.

Grail. The story of the Grail as told in Arthurian! legend is a christianized version of the legend appertaining to Amen! the magic cauldron! of Ceridwen,! and the great, the liquor which it distilled. This would not in any way invalidate its religious value but would remove it from the orbit of the Christian Church as having originated considerably earlier. There are possible links with Kvasirf and Soma.!
Grainne. In Celtic myth the betrothed of Finn,! who ran away with Diarmait.t The similarity of her name to Grannos! leads to the conclusion that she was originally priestess guardian of a mineral spring whose name has remained in myth while her function has been transformed in sex.
Grannos. Early continental Celtic god of mineral springs. Several localities in France are named after him, notably Aix-laChapelle (Aquae Granni), Graux, and Eaux Graunnes. An inscription to Grannos has been found at Musselburgh, near Edinburgh. The similarity of the name to that of Grainne! is worthy of attention.

Gylfi. An early ruler of Sweden who is used by Snorri in the Prose Eddaf as an excuse for the detailed outline of Nordic myth known as Gylfa-Ginning (Deluding of Gylfi). God ‘H.’ An unknown Mayat serpent god.
Hadad. Alternative form of spelling for Adad,t the Babylonian storm god. Hagen. In the Nibelungenliedt the murderer of Siegfriedt at the order of Brynhild.t He was later killed by Krimhild.t He also occurs in the Volsung Cyclet under the name of Hogni,t and in the Thidrek Sagat under his own name (Hagru).

Eammarsheimt. In Nordic myth ‘the Homecoming of the Hammer/ an alternative title for the Lay of Thrym/f one of the stories in the Edda.t Hanuman. In Vedic myth the general of the monkey-king referred to in the Ramayana.t He facilitated the assault on Ceylon by building a bridge or causeway still referred to as Rama’s, or Adam’s, Bridge, over which Rama and his troops crossed to rescue Sitat from the clutches of Ravana.t Hanuman would appear to have been the leader of some south Indian tribe who was immortalized for his bravery. Haokah. In Siouxt myth the thunder god, who employed the wind as a stick to beat the thunder drum. He wore horns showing that he was also a hunting god and had the gift of crying when he was cheerful and of laughing when he was unhappy, and of feeling heat as cold and cold as heat. Haoma. In Zoroastrian myth the Somat was worshipped under this name. It was considered as the purifier of the Place of the Sacred Fire, as the destroyer of demons and tyrants, and as the provider of husbands for spinsters.
Hap. Alternative spelling for Serapis,t the Egyptian bull god.
Hapi. Androgynous Egyptian god of the Nile, one of the four sons of Horns,t or sons of Osiris,t the others being Amset,t Qebhsneuf,t and Duamutef,t god of the north cardinal point and Canopic god protecting the lungs. As god of the Nile he wore a crown of papyrus plants in the north, and one of lotus plants in the south. His fertility was indicated by one pendent breast, while as a Canopic god he was ape-headed. The Nile, over which Hapi presided and which was called by his name, formed a part of the great celestial stream over which the boat of Ra,t the sun god, sailed daily. It encircled the earth, from which it was separated by high mountains. 90



Hati
Hara
At one place, however, was the throne of Osiris, near to a fissure
through which the waters reached the earth. The other end of this
aperture was said to be near the first cataract between two mountains near the islands of Elephantine and Philae. Owing to the dominating role played by the Nile in the life of Egypt, Hapi
gradually increased in importance until he joined the Great Company of Gods.f In the north he was figured as red with a bunch of papyrus; in the south with blue and lotus plants. The change in colour may be related to the dirtying of the waters of the Nile on its way northwards to the sea.

Hatif. A species of jinnt in pre-lslamic myth that is heard but not seen; usually communicates advice, directions, and warnings.

Havamal. ‘The Sayings of the High One.’ A poem in the Poetic Edda.t This collection of precepts, resembling parts of Ecclesiastes, contains one hundred and forty verses attributed to Odint and a runic section of some twenty verses of a later date. It is in this poem that Odin tells how he ‘hung on the tree for nine nights, wounded with a spear. ... I gathered up the runes . . . nine chants of power I learnt ... I won a draught of the famous mead.’
Hawaiki. In Polynesian! myth the traditional homeland of the Polynesians, from which they set forth to colonize the Pacific Islands. There are many assumptions ranging from India to South America for the situation of Hawaild, but none have as yet found full acceptance. An alternative spelling was Avaiki.t
Hawiyah. Seventh stage of the Islamic hell, Daru el Bawar,t a bottomless pit for hypocrites.

Hefeydd the Old. In Celtic myth the father of Rhiannon.t
Heimdal. In Nordic myth one of the Aesir.t The story of how he repopulated the world after some disaster is told in the Edda of Rig.t In a poem called ‘Heimdal’s Incantation’ in the Prose Eddat it is reported that he was the son of nine virgins, i.e. of a group of priestesses, which would show his royal descent. His function was to act as guardian of the Bifrostt bridge against the assault of the giants,t being in effect a warden of the outer marches.
He was famous for Gulltopprt his horse,t Hofudt his sword, and Gjallarf his horn. His traditional dislike of Lokif crystallized in a long-drawn-out series of combats for Brisingamen,t the neckace of Freya.t At Ragnarokt he finally defeated his enemy. His zodiacal housef was Himinbjorg, the name of his actual residence, on the borders of Asgardt at the end of the Bifrost bridge. 92

Hela. The goddess of the dead in Nordic myth. She was said to be the child of Lokit and to have been cast by Odint into Helheim, which Grimm thinks to have been a cavern. It is possible that Hela was a sibyl dwelling in a cave, an idea which is supported by Odin’s consultation with her about the death of Balder.t The comparison of her domain with the Christian hell is of recent date.
Helblindi. In Nordic myth the son of Farbautit and brother of Loki.t It was also a name given to Odin,t which seems to show' that it may refer to some important personage who existed earlier.

Herusmatauy Hiranyaksha
Herusmatauy. In Egyptian myth the son of Horust of Edfu and Hathort; also known as Ahy.t

Hey-Tau of Nega. Egyptian god of the Byblos region, known
from the period 3000 b.c. by Egyptian texts. He was transformed into a pine-tree, and can to some extent be equated with Tammuzt and Osiris.t Pepi I (sixth dynasty, 2400 b.c.), in his funerary inscription, compares himself to Hey-Tau, in his wooden sarcophagus. Others equate him with the Eshmunf of Byblos.
Kina. Alternative name for Sina,t the Polynesian moon goddess.
Hindu Creation Legend. In Hindu myth the world is now in its fourth Yugat or age. The first of these lasted 4,000 divine years, being preceded and followed by twilights of 400 divine years. The second lasted 3,000 divine years with twilights of 300, the third 2,000 with twilights of 200, and the fourth of 1,000 with twilights of 100 divine years. The four Yugas together cover a period of 12,000 divine years or 4,320,000 human years. This period is named a
Mahayuga.t A thousand Mahayugas make a day of Brahma, this being the length of time separating the Creation from the end of the world. The day of Brahma, or Kalpa,t is divided into fourteen periods, named Manvantaras,t each of which is ruled over by a Manu.t (As a thousand cannot be divided by fourteen without remainder, it is probable that the stories of the Manus belong to some different faith.) The present Manu is the seventh, which would put the world about mid career. See Vedic Sacred Writings.
Hinun. The thunder god of the Iroquoist Indians, who with the aid of his brother the West Wind overcame the Stone Giants,t the aboriginal inhabitants of the land.
Hiranyagarbha. In the Hindu Creation Legendt the primeval germ from which Brahmat was born. An alternative name is Narayana.t
Hiranyakasipu. In Vedic myth a demon of the Daityast related to Ravana,t who is slain by Vishnut in his fourth avatar as the man-lion, Narasinha.t

In Vedic myth a Daityat who occurs in NaraSinhat the story of the man-lion avatar of Vishnu.t He is also the villain of a Deluge legend akin to Hayagrivat in that he dragged the earth to the depths of the ocean. from the name having been a family one in the Daitya dynasty.

waited for the rays of the sun to dry up the mud banks left behind by the receding waters, and when the soil was in suitable condition

One sister lived in the east and one in the west; there was a third mother goddess, Ragno,t who lived by herself but did not take any recorded part in the proceedings. Horakhiti-Ra. In Egyptian myth a name of Horust on the Horizon. Another variant is Hermakhis.t These names are sometimes applied to Ra.t Horbehudet. In Egyptian myth a variant of Horus.t Hormazu. In Zoroastrian myth an alternative name for Ahum Mazda.t Horn. In Nordic myth one of the names of Freyat and also that of one of the members of the Asynjor.t Horn of Amenti. One of the boundaries of the Egyptian Elysian Fields. Horns. Drinking and hunting horns are listed under Treasures.t Horses of the Aesir. To the northern peoples the difficulty of securing horses as opposed to ponies must have been very great, as is shown bv the specific mention by name of all the horses that occur in the Eddas.t In the following are listed twenty of them: Alsuid, Arvar (the sun god), Blodighofi, Falhofnir, Gardrofa (dam of Hofvarpnir), Gils, Gladr, Glaer, Gultopprf (the horse of Heimdalt), Gyllir, Hamskerpir (sire of Hofvarpnir), Hofvarpnir (the horse of Gnat), Hrimfaxi (the horse of Nottf), Lettfeti, Silfrintoppr, Skeidbrirnir, Skinfaxi, Sleipnirt (the horse of Odint), Svadilfari, Synir. The horse of Balder was burnt on his funeral pyre. Horta. Etruscant goddess of agriculture from whom a town of Etruria derived its name. Horus. See Book of the Dead. Egyptian Religion. Son of Isist and Osiris,t nephew of Nephtyst and Set,t grandson of Nutt and Geat or Nut and Ra,t father of the four Canopict gods: Amsetyf Duamutef,t Hapi,t and Qebhsneuf/t and member of the Ennead.t There are several manifestations of Horus, which tend to overlap, and the problem of disentangling them is not always easy, as Horus may well have been the name of a whole series of predynastic rulers or priests. Another difficulty arises from the habit of the ancient Egyptians of combining two or three gods into dyadic or triune deities, which was frequently done with Amonyf Horus, Osiris, Ptah,t and Ra. The more important manifestations of Horus were: i. Horus the Elder, or Haroeris,t a falcon-headed sky god, of pre
dynastic origin, who may possibly have been the high priest or ruler of a tribe having a falcon totem. The story of his fight, as god of the sun, god of day, god of light, god of life and of all good, with Set, as 96



jjotei Hottentot
god of night, god of darkness, god of death and of all evil, was already
current in the earliest dynastic periods. According to this, Horus had two eyes, the sun and the moon, of which Set managed to steal
the sun, but was attacked by Horus, who inflicted a deadly wound
in one of his thighs. Thoth,t acting as mediator, made a treaty between them, allotting the day to Horus and the night to Set, and making them of equal length. Set, however, continued to persecute Horus, by cutting off pieces from his other eye, the moon, for a fortnight in each month, until there was none of it left. Thoth managed to frustrate him by making a new moon each month.
This is an interesting earlier variant of the Osiris-Set conflict, and seems to relate to a dispute between two sun and sky gods, Set having been a sun and sky god originally, combined with memories of some cosmic event of great import. Horus the Elder was known by many names, including Aroueris,t Horn Ivhent an Maa, Heru Khent Khat,t Heru Khuti, Hern Merti, Herunub, Heru Sam Taui, Heru Ur, etc.
2. Horus of Edfu, or Horbehudet.t A war god of Edfu, whose deeds were commemorated on the walls of the temple there.
3. Horus on the Horizon, or Herhkhty.t A manifestation of Ra, the sun god, on the horizon, i.e. the rising or setting sun. 4. Horus the infant sun god, who was reborn every morning, and was also a manifestation of Ra. 5. Horus the Child, or Harpakhrad.t The son of Isis and Osiris, who is usually shown as being suckled by his mother. He was conceived when Osiris had been brought back from the dead by Isis, a point which later allowed Set to oppose his claim for the throne of Egypt. 6. Horus the son of Osiris. He is sometimes shown as a man with a falcon’s head, wearing the double crown. He was the avenger of Osiris, and as such protagonist in the battles with Set, where we have another version of the story of the loss of an eye, only here Thoth brought it back and restored its sight by spitting on it. Horus then gave the eye to Osiris, who ate it and became filled with vital powers sufficient to enable him to take over the kingship of the dead. 7. Harpokrates. A son of a Horus god and Rat Tanit.t May be another form of Horus the Child, one of the Mendean Triad.t
Hotei. Japanese god of laughter and contentment. He is usually depicted as being extremely fat and carries on his back a linen bu.^ (ho-ki) from which he derives his name. He is one of the seven divinities of luck, the Shichi Fukujin.t
Hottentot Creation Legends. The majority of these appear to have been forgotten, but there is the early culture hero, Tsui Goab,t whose exploits were sufficiently important to be remembered.

Hou-Chi Hu
Hou-Chi. Early Chineset culture hero who at some stage became linked with the Chou dynasty. He was of royal descent, as is shown by the legend of his virgin birth, and was later raised to the rank of god for having brought the knowledge of agriculture to the Chinese, in which he resembles Shen-Nung.t
Houri (Hur). The damsels with ‘ retiring glances whom nor man nor jinn hath touched,’ who await the faithful in the Islamic paradise Dar el-Jannah.t
How-Too. Chineset earth monster god manifested in mountains and rivers. To him were sacrificed domestic animals, whilst prayers written on silk or parchment were buried before his effigy.
Hraesveglur. In Nordic myth a giant,t head of an eagle totem clan, who is referred to in the Prose Eddat as guardian of the gates.
Hrafna Galdur Odins. A somewhat obscure poem forming part of the Poetic Eddat entitled Odin’st Raven Spell.
Hrimthursar. In Nordic myth the ice giants,t sons of Ymirt or Hrim. Another version of the story in Ragnarokt refers, however, to the sailing of the hosts of the frost giants in the ship Naglfar,t steered by Hrim. Details are also given under Nordic Creation Legends.

Hringhorn. In Nordic myth the ship of Baldert which was described as being larger than either Naglfart or Skidbladnir.t After his death it was used as his funeral pyre, being launched for this purpose by Hyrrokint the giantess.t

Hsi-Yu-Chi. A record of a journey to the western paradise to procure the Buddhist scriptures for the Emperor of China. The work is a dramatization of the introduction of Buddhism into China, and contains within it many myths and legends, including that of Sun-Houtzu.t

Huginn (‘Mind’). In Nordic myth one of the raven messengers of Odin,T who was also a raven god, the other being Muninn.t
Huitzilopochtli. The humming bird wizard, the war and sun god, the chief god of Tenochtitlan, the Aztect city. He was the son of

Huixtoeihuatl. Aztect goddess of salt considered as the elder sister of Tlaloc,t a fact which would indicate that she may have been a pre- Aztec mother goddess. Her name resembles that of the absinthe plant.
Hunabku. The abstract, invisible, and supreme god of the Mayas ,f Was rarely worshipped in his own temples. Hun-Apu. Huncame. In the Quichet Creation Legend as told in the Popul Vuht he was the co-lord of Xibalba,t the cavern world. After murdering Hunhun-Aput and Vukub-Hunaput he and his fellow sovereign Vukubcamet were destroyed by Hun-Aput and Xbalanque.t
Hunhun-Apu. In the Quichet Creation Legend as told in the Popul Vuht the son of Xpiyacoc and Xmucane.t He and his brother Vukub-Hunaput were induced by a challenge to a ball game to enter Xibalba/f the cavern world of the Quiches, where they were murdered by its rulers. However, his two children by a Princess Xquiq,t Ilun-Aput and Xbalanque,t later avenged their father’s death.
Hunthaca. Moon goddess of the Chibchas.t She was originally the wife of their culture hero Nemquetcha.t In their Creation Legend it was told how in an access of fury she flooded the Cundinamarea Table Land so that only a few survivors escaped by reaching the mountain tops. The earth at that time being without a moon, she was transformed into the present-day Luna as a punishment, becoming confused with Chia.t
Huntin. An African tree spirit to whom fowls were occasionally sacrificed ; associated with the Kaffirs.f
Hurakan. Together with Gucumatzt one of the chief gods in the Quichet Creation Legend as told in the Popul Vuh.t He was a wind
god known as ‘the Heart of Heaven’ and vented the anger of the gods upon the first human beings by causing a deluge and a thick resinous rain which finally completed their destruction.

Huron Hyrrokin
Huron Indian Creation Legend. In the myth of the Huron North American Indians the two brothers Ioskehat and Tawiscaraf (who are the equivalent of Enigoriof and Enigohatgea) who were of virgin birth and the first leaders of the tribe after the Deluge quarrelled and fought. Ioskeha was successful and Tawiscara had to flee. Ioskeha made life possible on earth by defeating the Great Frog which had swallowed all the waters (i.e. the Deluge) and returning them to the rivers and valleys. One of the other Huron deities was Onniont,! the snake deity.
Huruing Wuhti. Two mother goddesses in the Hopif Indian Creation Legend. In fact they appear to have been two Deluge survivors who were the mothers of the ancestors of the tribe and who were assisted in their difficulties by Ragnofl who also occurs in the Pomot Indian Creation Legend.
Hutameh. Third stage of the Islamic hell Daru el-Bawar/f an intense fire for Jews.
Hvergelmir. In Nordic myth the fountain of Niflheim,! or Hela,1 from which flowed the Elivagart river.
Hy-Brasil. In Celtic myth a mysterious island in the Atlantic thought by some to be the last vestiges of Atlantis to which fled the leaders of the Tuatha de Danannf after their defeat by the Milesians.! Hy-Brasil was shown on maps of the Atlantic even after the discovery of America. Whether this is in any way related to the Nordic Yggdrasilt has not yet been determined.
Hymir. A Nordic giantt who was the father of Tyrt and the possessor of a magic cauldron.! In the Hymiskvida Edda! is told the storyf of the journey of Thor! and Tyr in quest of the cauldron, to bring it to the banquet of Aegir.t
Hyndla. The Hyndlu-Ljod Edda! poem is contained in the Flatey Book.! It tells how Freya,! mounted on her golden boar, invites the giantess Hyndla to ride on her wolf, and for them both to go to Valhalla.! The rest of the poem consists of the genealogical trees of several of the Norwegian dynasties, which are quoted by Freya or Hyndla as proof of their ancestry'. Inserted in the text is the shortened version of the Voluspa.t
Hyposouranios. One of the giants, child of Fire! in the Phoenician Creation Legend! of Philo Byblos. He was the first to build towns, while his brother, Ousoost was the first to make garments from skins.
Hyrrokin. In Nordic myth a giantess! who was called in by Odin! to assist in the launching of Hringhom,! the ship of Balder,! which was to be used for his funeral pyTe. When she succeeded in doing this single-handed, Thor! was so furious that he tried to
krill her.

Goddess * I.’ Usually presumed to be the Mayanf water goddess, represented in the Dresden Codex as holding an inverted earthen
ware vessel from which water flows. She may possibly be an equivalent of Chalchihuitlicue/f the Aztec water goddess, but lacking her benign character.
Ibe Dji. A special idol of the Yorubat tribes to commemorate the deaths of twin sisters.
Iblis. An Islamic name for devil, which comes from balas, a wicked person. The term is used nine times in the Koran, and in some cases the term is synonymous with Shaitan.t The name is sometimes applied to Azazil.f He was father of Sut,t and of the
Ghaddarf by a wife which Allaht had created for him. He was governor of the lowest heaven and of the earth. He was also known as Tausjt the peacock angel.
Ictinike. In Siouxt myth the son of the sun god who was expelled from heaven by his father for deceit and trickery and who is considered by the Sioux as ‘the father of lies.’ The stories told of his disputes with the beaver, the flying squirrel, the kingfisher, and the musk-rat, all totems of the Sioux, show that he was always defeated
by them, and he was probably a culture hero of some tribe whom the Sioux absorbed in the course of their history.
Iduna. The story of the Apples of Iduna which preserved the life and health of the Aesirf in Nordic myth may be a reverse side of the stealing of the Apples of the Hesperides by Hercules. They also seem to have formed part of the treasures of the Tuatha de Danann,t
for whom they were stolen by the sons of Tuirenn.f The tale of their theft, as told in the Conversations of Bragi,t is as follows : Lokit was kidnapped by Thiassit and obtained his release by promising to deliver Iduna into the hands of Thiassi. He enticed Iduna into the forest by saying he knew where she could obtain better apples
than her own. On entering the forest she was kidnapped by Thiassi. Without their daily supply of apples the Aesir grew old and grey, and it was only when they were at the point of death that they discovered what had become of Iduna. Under threat of punishment Loki was ordered to bring her back and, making use of the feather cloak of Freya,t he flew to the home of Thiassi and
rescued Iduna. He was pursued by Thiassi to the outskirts of Asgard,t where he was killed by the Aesir. Iduna, about whom
little is known except for her guardianship of the apples, was the daughter of a dwarf,t the wife of Bragi,t and one of the Asynjor.t IOI



Igigi Imberomba
Igigi. Babylonian spirits of heaven, as contrasted with the
Anunaki/f or spirits of earth. They may be the stars of the southern heavens.
Ikh. The glorified state of existence after death, as visualized by the Egyptians. For further details see Egyptian Religion.t
Ikto. In Siouxt myth the inventor of human speech and a being whose activities place him midway between the Egyptian Thotht and the Nordic Loki.t
II. Semitic name for god; similar to Elt; to be found in various combinations.
Ilah. Moon god of the southern Semites, similar to Ilmaqah.t
Ilamateeuhtli. Ancient Aztect fertility goddess known as ‘the Old Princess.’ She was originally linked with Mixcoatl/f the Cichimec god. Her secondary name, Citlallinicue,t ‘Star Garment,' may link her with the Milky Way. She was the lordt of the thirteenth hour of the day. Ilat. An early Arabian sun goddess, a variant of Allat,t and similar to Samas,t the Semitic sun goddess.
Illinus. Brother of Anuf and Ausf in the Babylonian Creation Legendt of Damascius. May possibly be Elt or Elat.t
Illiyun. The seventh heaven of the Islamic paradise Dar elJannah,t where was kept the register of the good deeds of all Moslems.
Ilmaqah. Semitic moon god, predecessor of Allah,t in preIslamic pantheon. He had a privileged place in the astral trinity, Ilmaqah, Sams,t and Atter,t being a god common to Semitism. From him may have been derived certain obvious essentials of Babylonian religion. Mosaic monotheism, and even Islam. In this particular pantheon the moon was masculine and the sun feminine, giving an indication of its great age, although even at that it may well have been preceded by a trinity of mother goddesses. He may be equated to Ilf or Ilah.t
Ilmarinen. A culture hero of the Kalevala,t the Finnish national
epic. He was the brother of Vainamoinen,t and the son of a
human mother, although bom on a hill of charcoal. He was a great smith and craftsman, and was described as a handsome young man.
Ilmatar. A heroine of the Kalevala/f the Finnish national epic; daughter of the air, creatrix of the world, and mother of Vainamoinen.f
Ilythyia-Leucothea. An Etruscant fertility goddess sometimes confused with Cupra.t
Imberomba. Culture heroine and first ancestress of the Kakadu tribe of Von Amlieim Land. 102

Imhotep
Imhotep. In Egyptian myth the deified minister of King Zozer (third dynasty). He was a patron of science and medicine and has been identified with Asklepios. In Greek the name is spelt Imouth.t He was one of the Memphis Triad! as the son of Ptaht and
Nut.t Imouth. Greek spelling of Imhotep.!
Imseti. In Egyptian myth an alternative spelling for Amset.t
In. In Japanese myth the Chinese dualistic principles Yangfand Yin,! male and female respectively, became In and Yo.t Details will be found under Koji-Ki.t
Ina. A name by which Sina/f the Polynesian! moon goddess, was known in Mangaia.!
Inari. Japanese god of agriculture whose shrines may be recognized by the two foxes which stand before them and who are said to be his messengers. The personality of Ukemochi,! an earlier mother goddess, has gradually been absorbed into that of Inari.
Inca Creation Legends. As the Incas were comparatively late arrivals in Peru, being the last of a whole series of pre-Columbian cultures, their Creation Legends are scanty in the extreme. Several of them are centred on Tiahuanaco,! where there are monolithic structures of great age far outdating the Incan culture. Here it was believed that both men and animals had been created, and the two mother goddesses of the lake, Mama Cochat and Copacati,! appear to have been connected with this. Later Titicaca became a centre of sun worship to which pilgrimages were made. In common with many other American races the Incas tell of a time when humanity sought refuge in a cave known as Pacarit from wliich came the founders of the Inca culture. Another story is that of Ataguju,! from whom descended Guamansuri,! the father of Apocatequilt and Piguerao.t Finally there was the mother goddess Mama Pacha,! from whom originated Pachacamac,! the earth god. Further details will be found under the following headings: Epunamun,t Guachimines,! Ka-Ata-Killa,t Mama Allpa,! Mama Oullo
Huaca,! Mama Pacha,! Mancocoapac,tPunchau,!Supay,tThonapa,t Tiahuanaco,! and Viracocha.t
Indcch. In Celtic myth a king of the Fomors! said to be a son of Dornnut who was later killed in battle by Ogma,! a chief of the Tuatha de Danann.t
Indra. In Vedic myth a god of battle and of rain. He appears to have been a real king, a jolly fair-haired fighting man of the Nordic type, who was deified after death, and whose worship spread at the expense of the older gods. Eventually, he was ousted by the
Brahmans in favour of Vishnu,! and sank from the position of a heaven god to that of king of a small portion of Swarga,! the Hindu 103



Infoniwoo Irin
paradise. His sacred city was Amaravati.t His title of Vrtraghna,! said to have been acquired by his defeat of Vritra,t may have been taken from the name of Vcrethaghnayt the Zoroastrian goddess of victory, whose name means the Slayer. In his military campaigns, Indra fought successfully against the Asuras,t the Daityas,! the
Danavas,t and the Dasyus.t He is frequently shown mounted on an elephant, named Airavata.t
Infoniwoo. God of Generation in the Formosan Creation Legend.t
Initiation Ceremonies. These ceremonies, no matter from what part of the world they originate, have' a tendency to resemble each other in outline. There are usually three stages: Katharsis, corresponding to baptism; Paradosis, corresponding to confirmation; and Epopteia, corresponding to ritual death and rebirth. The first two of these still exist in the Christian Church. The third stage, that of rebirth, is the period of meditation and temptation which all the great religious leaders of the past have had to undergo: Buddha sitting under the Bo-tree, Christ spending forty days in the wilderness. In many ceremonies the third stage included passage through a ring of fire, as with Brynhild,t Menglad,! and Skimir.t Innana. Early Babylonian mother goddess who later became merged in Ishtar.t She was also known as Ninni.t Both terms appear to have been generally applied to pre-diluvial goddesses.
Invisibility. Garments and rings producing invisibility are listed under Treasures.!
Io. Abstract supreme being of the Maori and the Polynesian peoples. In Tahiti the name is Iho-Iho or Io-i-te-vaki-naro. The resemblance to Iao, a name of Jupiter-Zeus, may be a coincidence. The fact that Io had receded into the background seems to show that he may have been an early god or culture hero of these races who was already in process of being displaced when they first arrived in the Pacific.
Ioi. Sister of Blue-Jayt in the Creation Legend of the Chinookst and participant in many of his adventures.
Ioskeha. In the Creation Legend of the Huront Indians, Ioskeha and Tawiscara,t his twin brother, were the founders of the human • race after Ioskeha had defeated the Great Frog which had swallowed all the waters of the earth. He brought to mankind the art of making fire and was the initiator of Huron culture. As a later development he and his brother became gods of the dayand of the night respectively. They are the equivalent of Enigoriot and Enigohatgea.
Irin Mage. Powerful magician who in the Tupi-Guarani Creation Legendt extinguished with a deluge the conflagration of the world caused by Monan.f Another version of this occurs in connection with Tawenduaref and Arikute.t 104

Irmin. Culture hero of a west German tribe, the Herminones. He may possibly be equated with Tiwaz.t The mysterious Irminsul, or ‘Column of the World/ which stood in a sacred grove near Marsberg, was destroyed by Charlemagne. There is a Teutonic word iarmund, meaning ‘great/ which may link Irmin with Jormungard,t the Midgardt serpent of Nordic myth.
Iroquois Indian Creation Legend. In the myths of the Iroquois tribes Athensic,t the ancestress of mankind, fell from heaven into the waters of the Deluge as it was receding and she found herself on dry land wliich soon became a continent. Later Enigoriot and Enigohatgea, the twin brothers, began to organize life again, and when the Iroquois migrated to the Land of the Stone Giants,t their enemies were overcome by the efforts of Hinun,t the thunder god, and his brother the West Wind. One of their later culture heroes was Atatarho.t In another version of the story the twins are named Ioskehat and Tawiscara,t as in the Huron Creation Legend.
Irra. In Babylonian myth a plague demon who at the instruction of Allatu,t Queen of the Underworld, tormented Ishtart when she visited it to find Tammuz.t
Ishtar. The Babylonian goddess of fertility. Her cult was first recorded in Erech, but probably started much earlier and spread to the whole of the Middle East, and even to Greece. On the Mediterranean coast she appears as Ashtart,t but without alteration of her essential characteristics. She was adopted into the pantheon of many races, and appears as the consort of Marduk,t Asshur,t Tammu7.,t and even as Ninlil,t consort of Enlil,t the storm god. She has also been identified with Damkina,t wife of Ea,f in which ' capacity she is the mother of Tammuz.t She was sometimes considered to be the daughter of Anut or of Sin,t while Frazer equated her with the Esther of the Old Testament. The story of her descent into Aralu,t the Babylonian Hades, to bring back Tammuz,t is told on a tablet in the British Museum. When she arrived at the gates she found them shut, and threatened to break them down to free the dead and to devour the living. On hearing this Allatu,t Queen of the Underworld, gave orders for her admittance. After performing the customary rites, which consisted in the removal of part of her dothing and ornaments at each of the Seven Gates, she arrived naked in the region of those ‘whose bread is dust, whose food is mud, who 1 105



Isis Isis
see not the light, who dwell in darkness, and who are clothed like birds in apparel of feathers.’ Allatu mocks her and orders Namtar,t the plague demon, to smite her with disease from head to foot. (This is obviously the description of an initiation ceremony in one of the early mysteries.) During her absence from earth, all fertility is suspended for man and beast. Shamash,t the sun god, receives the dread news through Papsukal.t He consults Ea and Sin, and Ea creates a being called Ashushu-Namir who is sent to Allatu to demand the release of Ishtar in the name of the Great Gods, a demand which Allatu could not refuse, so Namtar was ordered to bring Ishtar forth and to sprinkle her with the Water of Life. She was then conducted back through the Seven Gates and her garments and jewels returned to her. On her coming back to earth, life resumed its normal course. Ishtar occurs several times in the Epic, as befits the importance of her role as chief of the Igigi,t or spirits of heaven, and as the enemy of Gilgamesh. At some stage she absorbed Anunitumt and Nina.t She was the evening manifestation of the star Venus. With the Semites, the name was spelt Astarf or Istar.t
Isis. See Book of the Deadf; Egyptian Religion.t Egyptian goddess, daughter of Gebf and Nut,t sister and wife of Osiris,t sister of Nephtyst and Set,t and mother of Horus the Child.t One of the Ennead.t Although Isis, who was the prototype of the good wife and mother, is usually considered in relation to the myth of the murder of Osiris by Set, and her struggle to put her son Horus on the throne, in actual fact she would appear to have been a goddess in her own right, and possibly even before Osiris. Her magical powers were shown when Set caused a scorpion to sting her son Horus, and she managed to avert any evil result by reciting certain spells. On another occasion, when Horus and Set were fighting and had assumed the forms of huge black bulls, she was sufficiently powerful to slay them both. She could transform herself into any kind of creature, and travel through earth, air, fire, and water with ease. On one occasion, however, she wras defeated by Horus, who was so infuriated with her for releasing Set after a battle in which he had been captured that he cut ofi her head. Thoth,t however, magically changed it into the head of a cow and reattached it to her body. This myth arises from the identification of Isis with Hathort in some localities, and also from the fact that she was under the care of Hathor in the swamps of the Delta when rearing Horus. The lament which Isis and Nephtys were said to have sung after the death of Osiris was the official Egyptian funerary dirge. The worship of Isis spread far and vide, and images of Isis were found in
many parts of Europe. With the advent of Christianity these were 106



Isis Itzlaeoliuhqui
taken over as ‘Black Virgins/ of which there were still some half
dozen in 1939. In the same manner there was a Black Aphrodite in
Cyprus. _
On one occasion Isis desired to know the secret name of Ra.t This
she accomplished by collecting his saliva and forming it into a
poisonous snake, which caused Ra to become very ill when it bit him. As Ra had not created the snake, as he had the rest of the world, he
was unable to remedy the ill. Isis promised to cure him if he told
her his secret name, by which she would become all-powerful. The god tried to avoid the issue by telling her his other names, but to no
avail, and at the end he had to tell her and ‘it passed from his bosom
to hers.’ Isis was known by many names, including Aset,t Aust,t Eset,t
IIest,t Mert,t Selkit,t and Unt.t
Isis, Mysteries of. Egyptian religious ceremony which became widespread over the Mediterranean area, and which was still being performed in the sixth century a.d. They cover the death and resurrection of Osiris,t and the liiding of his coffin in a tree trunk at Byblos, from which Isis excised him. The best description is in Plutarch’s Dc hide et Osiride. A similarity to this ritual may be found in the Jewish ceremony of hiding the Passover cake known as Afikoman.f
Istar. Northern Semitic spelling for Ishtart as goddess of the evening star (Venus).
Italapas. In the Chinook Creation Legendt this name is given to Coyote,! to whom have been attributed a whole succession of good deeds, including the driving of the sea away from the prairie land so that men could settle down, and the laying down of the codes of taboos and hunting laws. The mischievous qualities of Coyote in other myths have in this case been attributed to Blue-Jay .t
Ith. A Milesian, the brother of Bile! and the son of Bregon.f
When he landed in Ireland at Londonderry he was murdered by the three kings of the Tuatha de Danann,t a deed which was followed by the Milesian invasion. He appears to have been a corn king of the Ivcmians and to have come to Britain from the Mediterranean.
The name survives in a Cornish cromlech called ‘ Grugith, ’ or Barrow of Ith, and also in the Cornish parish of St. Teath.

Ix. One of the four Bacabs,t the Mayat gods of the cardinal points. He represented the west and his colour was black. Ixazalvoh. Mayat goddess of weaving, wife of Kinich-Ahau,t the sun god.
Ixcuina. ‘Four Faces,’ an alternative name for Tlazolteotl,t the Aztect mother goddess. Ixtlilton. Aztect god of medicine and good health known as ‘Little Black Face.’ The priests of this god were medicine men or shamans and specialized in the treatment of children with the various medicines which they kept in stock.
Izanagi and Izanami. The first human couple encountered in Japaneset myth. Looking down from the floating bridge of heaven they stirred up the brine with a jewelled spear and the island of Onogoro arose from the expanse of waters. On it they settled and their children were Ama-Terasu,t the sun goddess ; Tsuki-Yumi,t the moon god ; Susa-No-0,t the sea god ; and Kagu-Tsuchi,t the fire god. After the birth of the latter child Izanami died, and her husband pursued her to Hades, where he found only her suppurating body, from which he fled in horror. This story is taken from the Koji-Ki,t the Japanese Creation Legend.

Jacy. In the Tupi-Guarani Creation Legend,t Jacy, the moon, is
the creator of plant life. As the moon under the name of Torugucnkett is the force of evil, it is possible the Jacy was originally some other celestial body, possibly the planet Venus. His fellow creator gods were Guaracyt and Peruda.f
Jagan-nath. In Vedic myth a vast idol, without legs, and having only stumps for arms, stated to contain the bones of Krisna,t which stands at Puri in Orissa, India. The peculiar shape of this monstrous image may be due to its being the irisida of a Buddhist tope, erected about 250. b.c. The term Jagan-nath means ‘Lord of the World/ and festivals are held in his honour, particularly in Puri, where as many as two hundred thousand pilgrims assemble for the occasion. At the Rath-yatra held in the month of Asarha, the temple car containing the images of Krisnaf and other gods is drawn through the town. In former times many devotees cast themselves beneath its ponderous wheels and were crushed to death. It is from this that the expression ‘beneath the wheels of the Juggernaut’ is derived.
Jahannam. The purgatorial or first stage of the Islamic hell, Daru cl-Bawar.t From this word comes the biblical term Gehenna.
Jahlm. The sixth stage of the Islamic hell, Daru el-Bawar,t a hot fire of idolators.
Jamshid. In Zoroastrian myth one of the earliest kings, and said to have reigned seven hundred years. He was killed by being sawn asunder by his enemy Zuhak.t He introduced cultivation of the vine and other useful arts.
Jann. In pre-Islamic myth the lowest, or fifth, species of jinn,t who had been transformed downwards for misdemeanours as some animals are transformed from men.
Jannatu el-’Adn. The fourth stage in the Islamic paradise Dnr cl-Jannah, the Gardens of Perpetual Abode, symbolized by large pearls.
Jannatu el-Firdaus. The eighth stage in the Islamic paradise, Bar el-Jannah,tthe Gardens of Paradise, symbolized by red gold.
Jannatu el-Ma’wa. The fifth stage in the Islamic paradise, Dar el-Jannah,t the Gardens of Refuge.
