Yaghutli. Pre-Jewish Arabian god, cognate with the XJz and the Jeush of Genesis. Is regarded as a personification of time.
Yahweh. Ancient moon god of the northern Hebrews ; may have
been an old Amorite deity. For a long time the name was interchangeable with Baal.t
Yakshas. In Vedic myth an alternative name for Rakshasas.f
Yama and Yami. In early Vedic myth Yama and Yami were a
hero king and his sister, or wife, rulers of a Valhallat of the Aryans, where the valiant would for ever enjoy the delights of a carnal paradise. Later, however, with the ousting of the early gods by Brahman importations, Yama gradually became grim and harsh,
finishing as the guardian of the hell of the Hindus. Yama was an Aditya.t In Chinese myth he was known as Yen-Wang.t Yama
and Yami were the children of Saranyu.t Yama corresponds to the Zoroastrian Yima.t
Yamilka. A story from the Arabian Nights relating, inter alia how the family of a serpent king were killed by the falling of a star from heaven. It is an early disaster myth which has survived by being incorporated in Arab folklore. There is also a marked similarity to the Gilgamesht story and the story of the Shipwrecked Sailor.t See also Serpent Myths.t
Yamm. Babylonian sea god killed by Baalt with the aid of two clubs fashioned by Rathar-Wa Hasis,t called ‘Driver’ and ‘Expcller.’ With the first he was struck on the body and driven from his throne; with the second he was struck on the head and driven from the seat of his authority. This may have been the ritual for deposing a god.
Yana Indian Creation Legend. Tins Indian tribe of the west coast
of North America have a myth which tells how a man in need of fire
saw a mountain emitting sparks. With four companions he went to
steal some fire from the mountain but on the return journey Coyote,!
who was carrying the fire, dropped it and everything in the world
was burned up.

Yang Yemaja
Yang. The Yang and the Yinf are the names given by the Chinescf to the opposing forces in a cosmological system of Dualismf which is said to have been invented by Fu-Hsi in the third millennium b.c. and to have been elaborated in the Yih-Kingt about
1122 b.c. While these dates are uncertain it may be said that by the time of the foundation of Confucianism! or Taoismt these doctrines were so old that their origin had almost been forgotten. The symbol of the Yang, the male element, is an undivided iine ( ) and that of the Yin, the female principle, a broken or divided line ( ). The combinations of these in pairs denote the heavenly bodies or the seasons, while the trigrams denote the elements or the forces of nature. With the degeneration of Taoism into polydemonism the Yang has developed into the ‘Shen,’ the gods of the earth and the air, while the Yin has become the * Kwei, ’ demons to be feared and propitiated. The Japanese equivalents are the Inf and the Yo.f Yangwu. The sun crow of Chinese! myth which may be equated with the Yatagarasu,! the sacred bird of the Japanese sun goddess Ama-Terasu.t
Yarih. Moon god of Ugarit, and lover of Nikkalt; he sent Hirihbi,! King of Sumer, to ask Baal! for her hand, offering 1,000 shekels of silver, even 10,000 shekels of gold, saying: ‘I shall send gems of lapis lazuli, I shall make her fields into vineyards, and the field of her love into orchards.’ This offer was accepted, thus fulfilling the prophecy of El! that Nikkal would be the mother of a wonderful son.
Yasna. The third part of the Zend-Avesta,! the bible of the Zoroastrian religion, containing liturgical works.
Yatagarasu. The eight-hand crow, the sacred bird of AmaTerasu,! the Japanese sun goddess. It may be identified with the Yangwu! of Chinese myth.
Yatis. In Vedic myth a group of holy men who were slain by Indrat and cast to the jackals. This story may indicate the failure of an early attempt by the Brahmans to put down the worship of Indra. The name is that of a present sect of the Jains. In the Zoroastrian! myth they were sorcerers.
Yazatas. In Zoroastrian myth the celestial beings to whom the Yashts in the Zend-Avesta! are addressed. Among the more important are Anahita,! Apo,! Asha,! Atar,! Haurvatat,! Mithra,! Rashnu,! Sraosha,! and Verethaghna.f
Yemaja. River and lake goddess of the Yoruba! people. She was the daughter of the earth goddess Oduduat and the sister and wife
of Aganju.t The child of this marriage was Orunjan,! god of the
midday sun. 238



Yen-Wang Yih-King
Yen-Wang. In Chineset myth the name given to Yama,t the
ruler of the Hindu other world. To the Chinese, however, his power
only extends to the lower spheres of existence, he has no power over
those who by their own striving after purity and goodness pass from
the realm of life and death directly to that of incarnate thought.
Yeti. In the Athapascant Creation Legend it was a great raven
with eyes of fire and wings whose noise was the thunder who de
scended to the flooded world and dragged the earth up to the surface.
He appears to have been the chief of a raven totem clan.
Yggdrasil. The world tree in Nordic myth. It was also the gallows on which Odint had hung for nine days in order to acquire wisdom. The elaboration in the Eddast of the memories of a sacred grove seems somewhat disproportionate and would in all probability be dissipated if there were access to earlier texts. The harts who were said to gnaw its shoots were named Dain,t Davalin,t
Durathror, and Duneyr, all of whom appear to have been dwarfs,
t
possibly the guardians of the grove. The squirrel Ratatosk,t which ran up and down the tree to breed discord between the eagle at the top and the dragon at the foot, may well have been a messenger between the guardians of the grove and its sacred fountain. The assumption that the powers of evil are beneath the roots of Yggdrasil is expressed in the Grimnis-Mal,t where it says, ‘Yggdrasil’s ash, more hardship bears, more serpents lie, under Yggdrasil’s ash, than simpletons think of; Goinn and Moinn, the sons of Grafvitnir, Grabak and Grafjollud, Ofnir and Svafnir.’ As both Goin and Moin are shown in the lists of dwarfs,t it seems probable that the other names mentioned are also of dwarfs. It is an interesting speculation whether Yggdrasil is in any way related to the Hy-Brasil of the Tuatha de Danann.t
Yih-King. The first of the nine authoritative works on Con
fudanism.t The name means ‘Book of Changes.’ The author was
a certain Wen Fang, the founder of the Chou dynasty, who reigned
about 1122 b .c. The work explains all the phenomena of cos
mology, nature, and human existence by diagrams made up of
straight lines arranged horizontally. These were said to have been
invented by Fu Hsi in the third millennium B.c. Of the explanatory
essays, half were written by Wen Fang, and the remainder by his son
Chou. The original signification of these sixty-four diagrams must
have been esoteric and magical and they are associated with the
ongin of the Yangt (the undivided line or male principle) and the
\ int (the divided line or female principle). The appendices include
the ideas of Confucius himself on these subjects. The following
work is the Shu-King.f 6

Yfma Yomi
Yim3. In the Zoroastrian Creation Legend.! Yima was warned of the Flood by Ahuramazdat and told to build a Yar, a cave or enclosure, in the Persian mountains in which representatives of all classes of things living should take refuge until the Flood was over. When the disaster had passed Yima sent the bird Karshiptaf to cany news of his safety to any survivors. The story that the
treasure of Yima is still hidden in a cave remains a part of modem Persian tradition. Yima was invited to carry out a religious reform similar to that of Zarathustra,! but refused to do so. Yima corresponds to the Yedic Yama.t
Yin. The female principle of Chinese! Dualism! as opposed to Yang,! the male principle.
Ymir. In Nordic myth a giant! who was father of the Hrimthursar,! the Frost! giants or Jotunn.t He was also known as Orgelmir.t He was killed by Odin! with the aid of his two brothers, Wilit and We,! and his body served to build the world, while his blood caused a deluge which drowned all the Frost giants except Bergelmirt and his wife, who escaped in a ship. In Ragnarok,! however, another version refers to the Frost giants sailing away in the ship Naglfar,! steered by Hrim, who may have been Ymir himself. Other details are given in Nordic Creation Legends,! Audhumla,! and Elivagar.f
Ynglingasaga, Yngllngatal. Names respectively of a prose recension by Snorri Sturluson and a (lost) panegyric by Thjodolf of Kvina in verse on the family called ‘the Children of Yngvi’ who became kings first of Uppsala in Sweden, then of Vestfold in Norway, and finally of all Norway. The mythological interest in the story is (i) the equation of Yngvi with Frey,! son of Njord.f (Compared with Odin,! Frey was not a fashionable ancestor in historical times: only
one English royal family—the East Anglian—traced their descent
from him, the rest from Odin); (2) the possible identity of the Ynglingar with Tacitus, Germania, ii; (3) the highly rationalized
account of Odin’s adventures in ‘ Asia’ = Asgard.f
Yo. In Japanese myth the Chinese Dualistic! principles Yang! and Yin,! male and female respectively, became In! and Yo. Details will be found under Koji-Ki.t
Yokut Indian Creation Legend. In the myth of this south Californian tribe it is told how in the beginning there was an island in the primeval ocean on which lived Eagle and Coyote,! who created men
and women to populate the earth. As there was no food for them they sent the dove to bring seeds for sowing.
Yomi. The other world of Japanese myth. It is presumably that realm which was ruled over by Emma-O.! 240



Yoruba Yum
Yoraba Creation Legend. The myths of this West African tribe centre mainly around the personality of Shangot, their first king, a leader of great ferocity, who lived in a palace of brass, where he kept 10,000 horses. It would appear that all their gods were tribal leaders at some stage preceding their arrival in West Africa and any other religion they may have had involving abstract gods has long since dropped out of use. Further details are given under Aganju,t Amirini,t Eda Male,t Ewuo,t Gwalu,t Ibe Dji,t Magba,t Obatalla,t Odudua,t Ogun,t Oko,t 01okun,t Oiorun/t Orishako,t Oro,t Orunjan,t Ose-Shango,| Oshalla/f Oya,t Shango,t Shankpanna,t and Yemaja,t
Yuga. In Hindu Creation Legendf we are living in the fourth Yuga (or age) of the present Mahayugaf (or epoch). The four Yugas are named Krita,f 4,800 divine years in length; Treta,t 3,600 divine years in length ; Dwapara,t 2,400 divine years in length; and Kali,t 1,200 divine years in length. Each divine year is 360 human years in length. If this latter exaggeration of the Brahmans is omitted, this would give a period of some 12,000 years since the Yedic races came into existence, which accords very well with the origin of the Hindu calendar in 11,500 b.c.
Yuh-Hwang-Shangte. Saviour of the world and lord of creation in Chineset Taoist myth.
Yule. A Nordic festival held on 14th January, when sacrifices were made to the Aesirt; it may have been the day of their annual meeting. The date was put back nine days to correspond to Old Christmas Day by order of Haakon the Good about 956.
Yum Kaax. ‘The Lord of the Harvest,’ a Mayaf agricultural deity who may be God ‘E.’t

z
Zabel of the Sea. Phoenician marine beast encountered by Khoserf in his fight against Baal.t This may be a memory of the battle between the sea and land religions for the domination of the coasts of Phoenicia.
Zamna. Alternative name of Itzamna/f the Mayat moon god. He was also known as Kabul.t
Zarathustra. By about six hundred years b.c. the Zoroastrian religion appeared to have reached its nadir, and had it not been for the work of Zarathustra, who reorganized it, it would doubtless have vanished. His writings have come to us in the Zend- Avesta,t which endeavoured to lay down a standard text for the stories forming the basis of the doctrine. He appears to have been a religious leader of the type of Moses, bringing the people back to their faith. In 520 B.c. Darius substituted the new monotheistic religion of Zarathustra for the polytheism then existing. In 485 B.c. an attempt
to suppress the new religion was put down by Xerxes. In 404 B.c. the old religion was incorporated in the new one.
Zarpanit. Babylonian goddess, wife of Marduk,t possibly a form of Aruru.t She was the Succoth Benotht of 2 Kings xvii. 30. She was also known as Ealur.t
Zarya. In Slavonic!- myth a beautiful lake or river priestesst of the healing waters who lived on the island of Bouyant near Alatuir,t the magic stone.
Zcernoboch. An alternative spelling for Czarnobog.t
Zehuti. Alternative spelling for Thotht in Egyptian myth.
Zend-Avesta. The Zend-Avesta, the bible of the Zoroastrian religion, originally consisted of twenty-one books, of which one whole book and a few fragments have come down to us. The work is divided into three parts, the Vendidad/f containing religious myth, laws, and the ‘ gathas ’ ; the Visperad,t a collection of litanies ; and the Yasna,t another liturgical work. Details of the myths are given under Zoroastrian Creation Legend.t The book itself appears to
have been made up some time subsequently to the seventh century b.c. The name Zend-Avesta is given to the Pahlavi translation of the original, on which most modem research is based.
Zerpanitum. Alternative spelling of Zarpanitt in Babylonian myth. 242

Zeus Demaros. His inclusion as a child of Ouranost and Geat

Ziudsuddu, or Ziudsuttu. The last of the ten Sumerian kings who reigned before the Flood. Zoroaster Zoroastrian
Zoroaster. Whether Zoroaster is another rendering of Zarathustra,t or whether he was the actual founder of the religion known under his name, is not clear. It is, however, reasonably certain that the religious doctrines of Zoroaster were definitely in existence for a considerable period prior to 600 B.c., when Zarathustra is said to have put the Zend-Avestaf into writing. Further details are given under Zoroastrian Creation Legends.t
Zoroastrian Creation Legends. The Creation Legends of the Zoroastrians are divided into stages. In the beginning there was Anahita,t the mother goddess, whose influence was so strong that even the Avestat had to recognize it. Then we have the shadowy background of Zervan Akarana,t the father of Ahura Mazda,
t
the power of good, and of Anra Mainyut (or Ahrimant), the leader of the powers of evil. The stories of the battles between Anra Mainyu and Ahura Mazda and their attendant hosts of angels or demons are similar to the Vedic stories of the fights between Indrat and the demons. They obviously refer to the struggles of the Indo-Geimanic races for a foothold in the Middle East. Ahura Mazda stated that the world would last for 12,000 years, a figure which recalls the 12,000 divine years of Brahma,t one thousandth part of a Kalpa.f One may, therefore, conclude that the figures were drawn from a common source, but that the Vedic estimates were carried many stages further. This period was divided into four stages of 3,000 years each, and Ahriman was unable to bring his counter activities to work until the first stage was ended. For the next three stages every good work of Ahura Mazda was matched by some evil one of Ahriman, and the battle will go on until the millennium, when Ahriman is defeated. The creation of living things took place from the limbs and body of the World Cow, and not a giant as in most stories. Finally, a man, Gayomart,t was created, only to be killed by the force of evil. After his death his twin children, Mashiat and Mashiane, were
bom (in some stories, as a shrub or tree), and from them humanity descended. At the time of the Deluge, Yima,t the patriarch, was warned of the disaster, and told to build a Var, or cave of refuge, in the hills, to which he and representatives of all living things would retreat until it was over. Later, however, Yima was defeated in battle by Azidahaka,t the king of a serpent-worshipping people. This disaster legend is of interest as it shows that the cave motif is not confined to the Americas alone. The worship of the sacred fire, which the Indo-Germans brought with them from their northern habitat, was carried to extreme limits under later Zoroastrianism. The guardians of the flame had to see that it was never defiled by the light of day, or by direct contact with
244



Zotzilaha Zuni
human beings. In the same manner the fear of defilement of the
sacred elements of earth, fire, and water led to the custom of dis
posing of the dead by placing the body at the top of a tower, similar
to the towers of silence of the modem Parsees. Here the body was
subjected to a process of weathering, if it escaped being devoured by
carrion eaters. Meanwhile the soul hovered around the body for
three days, and on the fourth ivas escorted by Sraoshaf to the
Chinvat Peretut Bridge over the abyss. Here it was judged by
Mithrat and Rashnu! and, after immediate penance for evil deeds,
proceeded across the bridge. This, however, is broad and pleasant
for the good, but narrow and impassable for the wicked, who fall into the clutches of the demons waiting beneath. This view of the future life represents a compromise between the Egyptian Hall of Judgment and the relative indifference as to good or
evil of the Vedas.t The Zoroastrian conception of Dualism,t with its balanced groups of good and evil powers, persists until to-day in various parts of the Middle East.
Zotzilaha Chimalman. Bat god of the Mayast who is probably the same as Camazotzt mentioned in the Popul Vuht of the Quiche Creation Legend.t The fear of bats manifested in the myths of the Central American races may be linked with the time when they were residing in caves such as those mentioned in the Aztect and Quiche Creation Legends and Nina Stahu,t the Cave of Refuge of the Blackfoot Indians.!
Zu. Assyrian storm god symbolized in the form of a bird. A
tablet (K3454) in the British Museum says that Zu once stole the tablets of Creation from Enlilt and took them to a mountain top. where he hid them. Adadt was chosen as champion of the gods but refused to fight for them. The rest of the tablet is missing, but in the_ legend of Etanat it is said that Shamasht eventually secured their return with the aid of a net in which Zu was ensnared. Zu’s role in this resembles that of Hayagravaf in the Yedic myths. He
may be equated with Garudaf or the Roc.t
Zuhak. In Zoroastrian myth the enemy of Jamshid/f the King of
Persia, whom he slew by cutting him in half.
Zume. Culture hero of the Paraguayan! Indians known as Kaboif
to the Karayas,! Kamut to the Arawaks,! and Tamu! to the Caribs.
He originated from the Place of Sunrise and to some extent resembles
Quetzalcoatl.! He may also be the Kamet of the Bakairif Caribs.
Zuni Indian Creation Legend. The myths of this tribe, which they
share with the other Pueblo Indians, tell how at the time of the
Uduge mankind sought refuge in deep caves. As the waters
receded Awonawilona,! their principal deity, caused the sun to play
245



Zunl Zufii
on the mists spreading over the waters, which dissipated, leaving behind aggregations of green scum which eventually, with the drying up of the flood, became the earth and the sky. After this Poshaiyankaya/f the founder of the tribe, made his way from the caves and found that with the vanishing of the Flood the world was a flat expanse of slippery mud which the sun father dried up, allowing mankind to live. At some time during the Deluge a boy and a girl were sacrificed and thrown into the water. Afterwards two rocks were known as Father and Mother in their memory.
246



APPENDIX
Abominable Snowman. For centuries there have been legends of strange man-like beasts to be seen in the snow-covered mountains of Tibet. The difficulty of access, the lack of oxygen and, latterly, the political situation, have made exploration of this area extremely difficult, at 20,000 feet and above but few mountain climbers are interested in sidelines, even as fascinating as this one. However, the latest reports from the area show that both British and Russian travellers are inclined to give more credence to the story than hitherto. The footprints of the being have been photographed and several expeditions have sought for traces in vain. The Tibetan name for the snowman is ‘Yeti/ while the females have been called ‘Ladni.’ And that is as far as investigation has reached at the moment. But it seems possible that here will be yet another example where a disbelieved legend turns out to be hard and solid fact. The first reports on the snowmen came from Beton, the surveyor of Alexander the Great’s Expedition to India, who stated :
1 Beyond the other Scythian man-eaters in a certain valley of Mount Imaus [the Himalayas] there is a region called Abarimon where live wild people with their feet turned round behind their legs, they are extraordinarily swift, and roam everywhere with the wild beasts. They are unable to breathe in an unfamiliar atmosphere and could not be taken captive to the neighbouring chiefs or to Alexander.’ The source of this is Pliny the Elder. The odd point is that the Tibetans of today believe that the snowmen have their feet pointing backwards, a fact which enables them to climb mountains speedily.
Asynjor. In Nordic Myth the women members of the hierarchy outnumber the men by two to one, or if the Valkyriest are included, by three to one. They met in Vingolf Hall of the Goddesses. The reason would lie first in the fact that Freyat and Frigga,f whose attendants they are, may be considered as being the same goddess, Frigga being her name with the Aesirt and Freya her name with the Vanir.t In consequence every one of the maids of Freya was duplicated by
’
one of Frigga. In addition the intermarriages with the giantst and
the dwarfs,t who may have represented the Finna and the Teutons,
meant another series of duplicates. Finally the Valkyries,t seem to have been the remnants of a 247



Aurva Brandan
matriarchal clan who were at some period absorbed into the Nordic
culture. The names of thirty-two of the Asynjor are given below, seventeen more are listed under Valkyries,t and three more under Norns,t making a total of fifty-two. Of these, as with the Aesir, it is unlikely that more than thirteen, i.e. Freya or Frigga plus twelve maids, ever functioned at any one time. Beda,t Bil,t Eir,t Fimla,t Fjorgyn,f Freya,t Frigga,t Frimla,t Fulla/f Gefjon,t Gerda,t Gna,t Hlina,t Hlodyn,t Hnossa,t Hom,t Jord,t Lofna, Mardoll,t Nanna,t Rinda, Sagayt Sif,t Siguna,t Sjofna, Skadi,t Snotra, Sol, Syn, Syr,t Vanadis,t Vora.
Aurva. A name given in Hindu myth to a great volcanic erup
tion of the past caused by the Rishi Aurva, who created from his thigh a great consuming fire which would have destroyed the world if Brahmaf had not assigned it to the ocean to a spot known as the ‘ mare’s mouth.’ The fact that this occurred in the sea shows that the story belongs either to the previous occupants of the Indian subcontinent or to the earliest times after the Hindus had arrived there.
Automata. The literature of the Near East and of the Mediterranean Region includes many stories of automata, usually made of brass, which were produced to the order of various potentates. King Solomon, who is reputed to have had a fountain of liquid brass in Andaluz—probably a foundry near the Rio Tinto Mines

which played three times a month, and from which were produced various novelties, including a brass throne with two eagles at the back which spread their wings to keep the sun off anybody sitting on it. Another one had two lions’ heads on the arm-rests which opened their mouths and made a noise when the king pressed on them. In the Arabian Nights there is the story of the City of Brass, in the middle of the desert, where mounted automata cut off the heads of anybody who approached them, and this in spite of the fact that there was nobody left alive in the city. Presumably they were activated by counterweights. These automata came into use when iron was replacing bronze for weapons, thus leaving the brass founders free to exercise their ingenuity on other things.
Brandan or Brendan. An apocryphal Irish voyager who was reputed to have lived in the fifth century. His tour of a whole series of mysterious islands is a hotch-potch of ill-digested information of earlier voyages put together by monks who had, in all probability, never been on a sea voyage of any extent and who did not realize that both climate and geography had to be taken into consideration. His visits enumerate twelve islands: (i) St Kilda; (2) Sheep Island, one of the Faroes; (3) Whale Islandt (as with 248



Churinga uorra
Sindbadt) ; (4) Isle of Parrots; (5) Isle of Monaskry; (6) Isle of Grape
Vines ; (7) Erupting Volcano—possibly Teneriffe ; (8) Isle of Wrecked
Ship; (9) Hermit Island; (10) An Iceberg; (n) A Seal or Walrus
Rookery; (12) Large Island with ripe fruit. (See also the com
parative list at the end of the Appendix.)
Islands Nos. 1, 2, 3, 5, 9, 10, and n belong to the climate of the
North Atlantic"; islands Nos. 4, 6, 7, S, and 12 to the South Atlantic
or even the Indian Ocean. The first batch may, possibly, have come within the ken of the Irish sailors and fishermen; the second
batch were most probably taken bodily from the stories brought back by pilgrims and travellers to southern Europe. The historical personage of this name, Saint Brandan of Clonfert,
is said to have been bom at Tralee in Kelly about a.d. 484. He was probably a Benedictine abbot.
Churinga. A name given to sacred tablets of wood or stone containing the souls of members of Australian! tribes. After death these tablets are hidden in trees or clefts until such time as a suitable mother for the reincarnation of the soul appears in the vicinity when the soul leaves the tablet and follows her to her home.
Corra (or O’Corra). The earliest of the legendary Irish voyagers, the others being Brandant and Maeldune.t While the total of islands was less, being but twenty-three, no fewer than sixteen of them also appeared in the other lists, as will be seen from the following: (1) Apple Island; (2) Island of the 4 Castes; (3) Island of

with Red-hot Spade; (12) Isle of Man with Red Horse; (13) Isle of Black Mourners; (14) the same as No. 6; (15) Iceberg; (16) Isle with Lacustrian Monster; (17) Isle of Monastery; (18) Isle of the Church; (19) Hermit Island; (20) Ant Island (the Formigas Rocks); (21) Isle of Screeching Birds; (22) Isle of the Cornmill; (23) Seal or Walrus

Culhwch Gog Magog
described as blacksmith’s forges. No. 12, the Isle of the Man with the Red Horse, might have been one of the Azores, where horses had been introduced by Carthage. No. 16, the Isle of the Lacustrian Monster, may be a distorted picture of a lagoon with a shark in it. Finally, No. 19, the Isle of the Church, is an addition of a later date.
Culhwch and Olwen. One of the few stories in the Arthurian cycle which is purely Celtic without embellishments from the Continent. The story itself is a typical one of a suitor having to undergo attacks and trials before winning his bride. The interesting part is that among the things which have to be produced are many of the treasures! of Britain. The cup of Lhvyr, the cauldron or basket of Gyddneu, the horn of Gwlgad Gododdin (or Bran Galed), the harp of Teirtu, the birds of Rhiannon, the cauldron of Diwrnach, the bottles of Gwyddolwyn the dwarf (a primitive thermos flask). One gains the impression that by the time that the story had been put into its present form the events referred to were so far back in the past that their original significance had been forgotten. However, one thing of importance emerges from it. At one time the
British Celts fought the Irish Celts and defeated them—‘ And when the hosts were utterly routed Arthur and his men went on board ship before their very eyes and with them the cauldron full of the treasures of Ireland.’ From this it emerges that Britain had no treasures until they had captured them from the Irish.
Duzzakh. The purgatory of the Zoroastrians, a place where the Devas might seize the soul of the sinner and plunge it into cleansing flames, which would, eventually, free it from Ahrimant the leader of the evil ones.
Fimbulvetr, the Great Winter of Nordic Mythology, when Hrimthursar,t the Frost Giants, bring with them the ice, while the Fire Giants pour down lava on the land before them. The real point of interest in this is that the tale goes back to the onset of the last great ice age which ended soon after 10000 b . c ., and must date back at least twenty thousand years before then. This does not imply that the Scandinavian races had spent the intervening period in northern Europe but rather that they had always dwelt in the most northerly latitudes and that when the ice age set in they had been obliged to retreat southwards in order to survive. And, later, as the ice glaciers receded, the ancestors of the Norsemen moved northwards and occupied the lands vacated by the ice-fields. The ensuing disaster, Ragnarok,t completes the destruction of the first Nordic culture.
Gog Magog. The giants of this name mentioned by Geoffrey of
Monmouth, and whose effigies were kept in London, have a history which seems to go back to Britain in the Iron Age or even possiblv 250



Gog Magog Gog Magog
chariot and horses, and accompanied by a later period giant com
to the Bronze Age. Magog seems to have been the goddess and Gog or Og her husband or son, of a moon- worshipping, horse-using
race such as the Cimmarians who migrated on horseback.
Og, the giant King of Bashan, may have been the high priest of a Moon God, while from the words of Ezekiel it seems that the
' Hordes of Gog and Magog’ were mounted on horseback. Josephus considered them to be Scythians, a description which included the Cimmarians. Fenius Farsa, the founder of the Milesian race, a grandson of Magog, was described as a Scythian.
However, by the time that the cult had reached Britain, Magog was the great mother goddess associated with a horse cult, memories of which still persist in the ride of Lady Godiva through Coventry,
clothed only in her hair, and in the Lady of Banbury Cross, who had rings on her fingers and bells on her toes. The horse had to be white, although on certain occasions the woman riding it blacked herself all over. The White Horse of Uffington seems to have served as a model for the horse on early British coins, with its beak, disjointed legs, and, in the case of a coin found at Carn Brea, four teats discharging milk, and was the emblem of a fertility cult. The White Horse at Bratton, the earlier one, not the present copy, seems to have been improved to an extent where it lost its original shape. The point of these observations is that the White Horse symbol appeared in Britain before the time of Alexander the Great, and the coining of the first stater with its horse on the reverse, from which the early British are presumed to have copied their coins. Why should they do so when they had a perfectly good horse of their own which was also a well-known religious symbol? Recently a Cambridge archaeologist, Mr T. C. Lethbridge, discovered on Wandlebury Hill, adjoining the Gog Magog Hills, a most remarkable hillside figure of a goggle-eyed goddess complete with plete with raised sword, and a sun god of equal size. Both these seem to represent the gradual displacement of the mother goddess by the father gods, but the popular belief in the old gods must have been too strong for their complete effacement. There arc two other hill figures in Britain which may be contemporary with the Uffington Horse and the Wandlebury Goddess.
These are the Long Man of Wilmington, holding in each hand what appears to be a long staff, about 140 feet in length, but which may represent the doors separating day from night ; and the Ceme Giant, a Hercules type, holding an upraised club. Photographs of both of these give indications of other shadowy figures, now’ almost ob
literated by vegetation, which may fit into the Gog Magog theory.
The dates of these figures have been put as between 4oo°b.c. and
A
-I) - 43 ; rime of the first Roman invasion of Britain. It is at 2ql



Guanche Maeldune
least a possibility that the invasion of Britain by the Trojans recounted by Geoffrey of Monmouth, with the overthrowing of Gog Magog at Plymouth, might be a memory of the Belgae attacking and overthrowing the worshippers of Magog, their predecessors as emigrants across the Straits of Dover. It is known that there was a hill figure of a giant at Plymouth winch is now overbuilt. The architectural skill involved in laying out these hill figures must have been considerable, as in none of them is there any place within reasonable reach where it is possible to see the whole figure at once. The appreciation of the modem generation of these features of the landscape has only become possible thanks to air photography or the use of long-range lenses. One can only suppose that a master plan was made, and from it the actual figure laid out, possibly by dividing it into numerous small squares. While the worship of Magog, in one form or another, was known throughout western Europe, the cutting of these liillside figures was peculiar to Britain, as also the ceremonial processions of a priestess or representative of the goddess, riding naked on a white horse, while the populace were sternly enjoined to keep within their doors to avoid a breach of religious taboo. The picture of religious life in early Britain is gradually evolving but is still incomplete.
Guanche Theogony. The Guanches of the Canary Islands appear to have been refugees from the mainland who exterminated the original inhabitants. They had an all-encompassing male deity named Abora in Palma, Achaman in Teneriffe, Acoran in Gran Canary, and Eraoranzan in Teneriffe. The Gran Canary name shows marked traces of Muslim influence. There was also a goddess named Monieba who lived in Hierro, and a devil named Guayota whose hell was the volcano of Teneriffe, which was named Echeyde. The Guanches practised mummification, a trait which they had in common with the Egyptians. They also did not know of the wheel, employing sleds for transport.
Hvergelmir. In Nordic Myth twelve rivers flowed from the fountain of Hvertelmir in Niflheimt or Hela.t These were: Fimbul/f Fjorm, Gjoll, Gunnthra, Hrith, Leiptur, Slith, Svaul, Sylgil, Thulr, Vith, and Ylgr. As, with the exception of Fimbul,t these names occur nowhere else in the Eddas, it is presumed that they must represent the oldest geographical memories of the pre ice-age era. The twelve rivers are together known as Elivagar.
Maeldune. A legendary Irish traveller akin to Brand ant and
Corra.t He is stated to have visited no less than thirty-three islands of which it has only been possible to identify a few. The list is as follows: (i) Murderers’ Island; (2) Ant Island (the Formigas Rocks); (3) Isle of Terraces and Great Birds; (5) Isle of Horse with 252

Undersea Island (a Lagoon in West Indies?); (24) Monkey Island with coconuts; (25) Island with waterfall and salmon; (26) Island with silver column (this recalls the Findrinef of Corra); (27) Island standing on a pedestal (the mushroom volcanic cloud during an eruption, also mentioned by Corra); (28) Island with nunnery of eighteen women; (20) Island of Red Berries; (30) Island of Sheep and an Eagle, with which is combined fountain of youth (possibly one of the Faroes or Shetlands); (31) Isle of Revellers (probably a monkey island, similar to that of Sindbadt; (32) Island with monk and hawk (probably St Kilda). The voyages attributed to Maeldune range from the tropics to the Arctic, from the West Indies to the Canaries, they even include places visited by travellers in the orient. The Azores and places north, and even the West Indies may have been within the ken of the Irish travellers, but it was unlikely that they would venture as far south as the Canaries. There is always the possibility that the mushroom cloud may have been St Pierre, Martinique, in eruption. Nevertheless the descriptions may be taken to be those of actual places visited at one time or another by some sea venturer. In considering these early voyages it must be remembered that the total number of islands which could have been encountered in the Atlantic Ocean by mariners was small. There were 10 in the Azores, including the Formigas; 8 in the Canaries; 6 in the Cap
Verde—assuming that sailors got so far south; 4 in the Faroes; and
4jn the Madeiras. Adding Greenland, Iceland, Rockall, and St. Kilda, the total is 36. As it is unlikely that any traveller visited
them all, the figure should be reduced by half, which brings it well within the lists of the three Irish travellers. It may be taken that
any stories of the Mediterranean or further east are third or fourth
hand. The coastal islands, Hebrides, Shetlands, and the like, are not
included, as to go there was a normal voyage and not an adventure.
See the comprehensive list of islands at the end of the Appendix.
Merlin’s Mirror. A magic mirror, said to have been invented by 253



Nine Og
Merlin,! which gave warning of the advent of an enemy. It may have been some form of camera obscura.
Another version of the talc says that Merlin made two brass images of men which would throw a ball from one to the other to arouse the citizens. This, however, appears to be but one of the automatat so common in the literature of the Near East which has managed to become inserted in Arthurian myth. The mirror resembles that reported by Benjamin of Tudela as standing in the Pharos at Alexandria.
Nine Worlds. In Nordic myth, the universe was divided into nine sections as listed below: 1. Ljosalfaheim, the heaven of the righteous, separated by Vidblainn from 2. Muspellt heim, the home of the flame spirits led by Sutur,t separated by Andlang from 3. Godheim, the dwelling of the Aesir.t 4. Vanaheim, the dwelling of the Vanir.t 5. Mannheim, the abode of mankind. 6. Svartalfheim, the cavern world where dwell the elves. 7. Jotunt heim, the land of the giants. 8. Helheim, the region of death. 9. Niflheim,t the lowest region in which dwelt also Nastrond. It is interesting to note that the Aesir insisted on retaining their social status vis-a-vis the Vanir, even although the two races had fused into one.
Odinic Trinity. The Trinity of Nordic Myth, consisting of the High, the Equally High, and the Third, referred to in the second chapter of the Prose Eddaf; their names were Har, Janfar, and Thridi, in ascending degree of importance.
Og. The giant King of Bashan, from whose name we may have acquired the word ‘ogre’ coined by Charles Perrault to describe man-eating giants. The name is frequently paired as Gog Magog,
t
when Magog was the Moon Goddess and Gog or Og her spouse or son. The ogre killed by Jack of the Beanstalk originally had three heads, in a similar manner to Svantovitt the Slavonic! god. Dr. Margaret Murray has noted that the ogres were not only very tali but big boned, having a broad face with coarse features, high cheek bones, lumpy nose, wide mouth with large teeth, and a coarse, straight, and straggly beard. All of these are characteristic of the Ugrians of south Russia who penetrated to Europe and the Near East from the Black Sea. In addition they were cannibals until Roman times, as were their kindred the Scyths. Og, the King of Bashan, may well

Olwen. In Celtic myth she was the May Queen, the daughter of
the May tree, or the Hawthorn, or the White Thom. Her story is told in the romance of Kilwycht and Olwen, in which Peredurt the
Knight is also encountered. She may be equated with Blodeuwedd .f She was the daughter of Ysbadadden, a King of the Giants, whose life would come to an end on her marriage. In consequence he places every possible obstacle in the way of the prospective bridegroom, but in the end he fails. This is in accordance with the classical tradition.
Orwandil. Name of a giantf who was defeated in battle by Thor,t who threw one of his toes into the sky, where it became a star. In Britain the giant is commemorated by a figure cut in the side of the Gog Magogt hills at Wandlebury, where he is said to have stolen the spring so that winter grew longer and longer until it seemed that the world must die. Eventually he was caught by the gods and cast into the sky, where he became a star. This cosmic myth recalls that of the Frost Giantst and the Fimbult winter of the Eddas.f Wandel may have been the war god of the Iceni who was displaced after their defeat by the Belgae.

in the Shah Nameh of Firdusi. The wars described in this work
belong to the period when the Persians were battling against the Indians on the east, the Caucasians on the north, and the Semites on
the west. The period is some time after the arrival of the Indians in India, but not very long, as the Hindu gods have become the
Persian demons and vice versa. Rustum and his mare Rakush, apart from many other adventures, 255

Of these the Whale Island was the subject of three stories of St.
Brandan; the Roc and the Valley of Diamonds seem to have been
known to every writer of oriental antiquity; the Isle of the Cannibals
was also well known; the Cyclops is a story which Homer must have
picked up from the East, it seems to be related to the Gog Magogt
tradition; the burial of the husband with the wife was practised in

which is still told in Africa and Asia and which seems to retain in it

The Island of the monkeys was also referred to by Maeldunef as

Stone of Destiny. This stone, which forms a part of the Coronation Chair at Westminster Abbey, was brought from Scotland by Edward I after defeating the Scots in 1296. It had been the crowning stone of the Scottish kings and was said at one time to have been the Chair of St. Columba at Iona. However, in actual fact it seems to have been taken by the Scots from Ireland, where it had been known as the Stone of Tara and, earlier still, as the Lia Fal which had been brought from Falias.t It was Walter Scott who said:
‘ Unless the fates are faithless grown, And prophet’s voice be vain, Where’er is found this sacred Stone, The Wanderer’s race shall reign.’
Actually there are several other sacred stones of a similar nature still extant in Britain. There is the London Stone in the City on
which Wat Tyler was proclaimed king; there is the stone at Kingston
upon-Thames on which the Saxons crowned their kings. These stones
seem to be peculiar to the British Isles.
Terrestrial Paradise. The possibility of rediscovering the lost
paradise of mankind inspired explorers for centuries. Perhaps the
earliest reference to it occurs in the Peregeisis of Prescian, itself a
rendering of an earlier work by Dionysius, that is apart from the
iblical v ersion. The earthly paradise was always placed just out
side the region known to man at the time, but the very nature of the
descriptions tend to show that at one time the ancestors of the
Aryan and Semitic races lived under climatic conditions which can
only be described as more favourable than any they have enjoyed
since then. Their enforced exile from there dates back to the
beginning of the last ice age when tropical areas became icy wastes
" hen meadows became and deserts, when the bitter struggle for
25 7



Tibetan Tibetan
existence turned friends into enemies and whole nations into dispossessed refugees wearily making their way towards a climate in which they could survive. The fact of sudden climatic changes is shown by the mammoths in Siberia, who were suddenly wiped out with undigested meals in their stomachs.
Tibetan Lamaism. The religion of Tibet, in theory a branch of Buddhism, was in reality a primitive dcmonolatry including human sacrifice and still retaining memories of ritual cannibalism. The introduction of Mahayana Buddhism in the sixth century seems to have done no more than apply a superficial coating of conformity. An interesting relic of the dualistic system of control is to be seen in the Dalai Lama, who is the religious head, and the Panchen (Tshai) Lama, who is the secular one. Originally this was not the case but the political events of the last twenty years have precipitated the reversal. The Lamas themselves are the higher ranking members of the priesthood down to as far as Abbots. There is a hierarchy of Chutukus or Cardinals who normally assemble once a year at Lhasa for the Feast of Lights. Most of these are presumed to have been reincarnated and, as such, bear the title of ‘ Rimpoche.’ The pre-Buddhist religion was known as Dugpa and its followers are mainly concerned with the practice of magoc, or Tantrism, based to some extent upon Hindu writings. One of these is the Yogachchara Bumi Sastra. The method involves the repetition of phrases and gestures and the use of yantras, magic diagrams, composed of triangles. Healing is also part of the functions of the Dugpa Lamas and involves the use of a method similar to acupuncture. The Prayer Wheel of Lamaism has written on the outside: ‘Oml Mani Padme Hum!’ whilst within it are contained sacred texts, written many times, and packed into a small space. Each time the wheel is turned it equals the repetition of the prayer as many times as it is written. Tibetan myth tells of two demons, Matram and Krodish, who were slain in battle by two Bodhisattvitas, or potential Buddhas. This illustrates the difficulties inherent in superimposing a religion which allows for no specific named personalities upon one where every demon or god had a name and a definite part to play. An interesting aspect of Lamaism is that any form of digging in the earth was considered as being likely to arouse the enmity of the demons of the lower earth. The consequent dislike of outsiders who tend to overlook this has been an important factor in the dislike of Tibetans for all foreigners, recently especially so in respect of the Chinese, who are understood to be mainly interested in exploiting the mineral wealth of the area. The centre of Lamaism is the Potala, the Palace of the Dalai 25S

Whale Islands Whale Islands
How and by what means the secret penetrated to the short,
thickset, swart* dwellers in the Black Forest, the dwarfs and trolls of Germanic legend, is not certain, possibly along the amber routes which passed near by. What is certain is that the fairy sword makers of northern Europe were mainly centred there where ample supplies of charcoal and surface iron ore were available. The Kalevalat gives a remarkable description of the forging of a magic sword in Runo 9. Here Ilmarinyf the Smith, forges the Sampo and breaks up his melt no less than four times to cast it back into the furnace before being satisfied with the result. In the same way Weyland made two magic swords, one for the Aesir,t Mimung, and one for Beowulft which he used to slay the dragon. The difference between a magic sword and an ordinary one was that the magic sword would actually wound by cutting as opposed to the sheer weight of the blow. The secret lay in the use of high carbon iron, a good charcoal fire, repeated hammerings and heatings, and, finally, the quenching of the weapon in water or other liquid. Gilibur, the sword of Arthur, was drawn first from a black block, presumably of carbon and slag, and subsequently quenched in the waters of a lake. The process was known as false damascening or pattern welding. The swords of the Varangian invaders of Russia were thus made by Scythian smiths. The tradition of the sword makers was that the raw block of metal was left near the forge with a sum of money and that a day or so later the finished sword would be found waiting on the same spot. Except in the case of royalty there seems to have been no personal contact between the smiths and the buyers, possibly because they did not speak the same language. This may simply have been because fighting men felt the work of a smith undignified. The fame of Weyland was shown by King Alfred the Great’s reference to him: ‘ Where are the bones of Weyland now, and who knows where they may be? ’ However it would seem possible that the name 4 Weyland the Smithf’ may have been handed down from father to son, or master to apprentice, for a long period of time until at length the craft of smith became accepted as a normal profession. When Weyland’s Smithy on the Ridgeway was excavated in 1921, two Iron Age currency bars were found, showing that the tradition had been in existence at least since then.
Whale Islands. One of the most interesting of the earliest travel stories is told in the first voyage of Sindbad the Sailor, t in which he
recounts how he and his companions disembarked on a small island and were enjoying a meal when suddenly the island trembled and, plunging into the sea. turned out to be a whale.
An Arab writer, El Kazwene, tells a similar story of a huge water tortoise, while Olaus Magnus described mariners as frequently 260



Whale Islands Whale Islands
disembarking on whales and cooking their meals there under the
impression they were on land.
Brandan. the early Irish traveller, not only saw one of these islands but visited it "several times, on the last occasion celebrated
Mass on it, a statement which puts the whole thing slightly out of context as the voyage referred to must have taken place centuries before the Christian Church had been founded. However, it is interesting to note that the story was known from one end of the Old World to the other and to speculate whether it might not have been based on the second or tortoise avatar of Vishnu,t when on his back was placed a mountain. To the inhabitants of India and the southern seas a tortoise was a usual sight, whilst to the tellers of the Arabian Nights, and to the European writers such as Olaf Magnus, and the tellers of the legendary Irish voyages, a tortoise must have been unknown, for which reason it was replaced by a whale. Naturally there have been instances when sailors climbed on the back of a whale, but that they should assume it to be an island seems out of the question.
